volume I





 who wisdom's sacred prize would win,

 must with the fear of God begin;

 immortal praise and heavenly skill

 have they who know and do his will.

                            New Version.









CHAPTER I.



CHARLOTTE.



  farewell reward and fairy,

  good housewife now may say,

  for now foul slut in dairy

  may fare as well as they.

                     BP. CORBET.





an ancient leafless stump of a horse-chesnut stand in the middle of a

dusty field, border on the south side by a row of house of some

pretension.  against this stump, a pretty delicate fair girl of

seventeen, whose short lilac sleeve reveal slender white arm, and

her tight, plain cap tress of flaxen hair that many a beauty might

have envy, be bang a cocoa-nut mat, chant by way of

accompaniment in a sort of cadence--



 'I have find out a gift for my fur,

  I have find where the wood-pigeon breed;

  but let I the plunder forbear,

  she will say--'



'hollo, I'll give you a shilling for 'em!' be the unlooke-for

conclusion, cause she to start aside with a slight scream, as there

stand beside she a stout, black-eye, round-face lad, his ruddy cheek

and loutish air show more rusticity than agree with his keen, saucy

expression, and mechanic's dress.



'so that's what you call beat a mat,' say he, catch it from her

hand, and mimic the tender clasp of her little finger. 'D'ye

think it's alive, that you use it so gingerly?  look here! give it he

well!' as he make it resound against the tree, and emit a whirlwind of

dust.  'lay it into he with some jolly good song fit to fetch a stroke

home with!  why, I hear my young Lord say, when Shakspeare be a

butcher, he use to make speech at the calf, as if they be for a

sacrifice, or ever he could lift a knife to 'em.'



'Shakspeare!  he as write Romeo and Juliet, and all that!  he a

butcher!  why, he be a poet!' cry the girl, indignantly.



'if you know well than Lord Fitzjocelyn, you may!' say the boy.



'I couldn't have think it!' sigh the maiden.



'it's the good of it!' cry the lad, eagerly.  'why, Charlotte, don't

ye see, he rise hisself.  anybody may rise hisself as have a mind to it!'



'yes, I've read that in book say Charlotte.  'you can, man can, Tom,

if you would but educate yourself like Edmund! in the _old English

Baron_.  but then, you know whose son you be.  there cann't be no

catastrophe--'



'I don't want none,' say Tom.  'we be all equal by birth, so the

orator prove without a doubt, and we'll show it one of these day. a

rare lady I'll make of you yet, Charlotte Arnold.'



'o hush, Tom, I can never be a lady--and I cann't stand dawdling

here--nor you neither.  'Tisn't right to want to be out of our station,

though I do wish I live in an old castle, where the maidens work

tapestry, and hear minstrel, never have no stair to scour. come, give

I my mat, and thank you kindly!'



'I'll take 'em in,' say Tom, shoulder they.  ''tis breakfast-hour,

so I think I'd just run up and ax you when my young Lord go up to

Oxford.



'he be go,' say Charlotte; 'he be here yesterday to take leave of

missus. Mr. James go later--'



'gone!' cry Tom.  'if he don't say he'd come and see I at Mr.

Smith's!'



'do you want to speak to he?'



'I want to see he particular.  there's a thing lay heavy on my

mind.  you see that place down in Ferny dell--there's a steep bank down

to the water.  well, my young Lord be very keen about build a kind

of step there in the summer, and he and I settle the stone, and I

be to cement 'em.  by come Mr. Frost, and find fault, what I

think he'd no call to; so I fling down my trowel, and wouldn't go on

for he!  I be so mortal angry, I would not go back to the work; and I

believe my Lord forget it--and then he go back to college; and

Frampton and Gervas, they put on I, and you know how 'twas I come away

from Ormersfield. I be not go to say a word to one of that lot! but

if I could see Lord Fitzjocelyn, I'd tell he they stone arn't fix;

and if the frost get into 'em, there'll be a pretty go next time

there's a tolerablish weight!  but there--it be his own look-out!  if

he never think it worth his while to keep his promise, and come and

see me--'



'o Tom! that ben't right!  he only forgot--I hear Mrs. Beckett tell

he he'd forget his own head if it ben't fix on, and Mr. James be

always at he.'



'forget!  Aye, there's nothing gentlefolk forget like poor folk. but

I've do with he!  let he look out--I keep my promise to he long

enough, but if he don't keep his'n--'



'for shame, for shame, Tom!  you don't mean it!' cry Charlotte. 'but,

oh!' with a different tone, 'give I the mat! there's the old Lord and

Mr. Poynings ride down the terrace!'



'I ben't ashamed of nothing!' say the lad, proudly; and as Charlotte

snatch away the mat, and vanish like a frightened hare, he stalk

along like a village Hampden, muttering, 'the old tyrant shall see

whether I'm to be trample on!' and with both hand in his pocket, he

gaze straight up into the face of the grave elderly gentleman, who

never even perceive he.  he could merely bandy glance with Poynings,

the groom, and he be so far from indifferent that he significantly

lift up the end of his whip.  nothing could more have gratify Tom,

who retort with a grimace and murmur, 'don't you wish you may catch

I?  you jealous syc--what be the word, sick of uncle or aunt, be

it, that the orator call 'em?  he'd say I'd a good miss of be one

of that sort, and that my young Lord there open my eye in time.  no

well than the rest of 'em--'



and the clock strike eight, he quicken his pace to return to his

work.  he have for the two or three previous year be nominally under

the gardener at Ormersfield, but really a sort of follower and

favourite to the young heir, Lord Fitzjocelyn--a position which have

bring on he dislike from the superior servant, who be not

propitiate by his independent and insubordinate temper.  fault on

every side have lead to his dismissal; but Lord Fitzjocelyn have place

he at an ironmonger's shop in the town of Northwold, where he have be

just long enough to become accessible to the various temptation of a

lad in such a situation.



Charlotte sped hastily round the end of the block of building, hurry

down the little back garden, and fly breathlessly into her own

kitchen, as a haven of refuge, but she find a tall, stiff starch,

elderly woman stand just within the door, and hear she last word.



'well! as I say, 'tis no concern of mine; only I think it the part

of a friend to give you a warning, when I see it with my own eyes!--

ah! here she be!' as Charlotte drop into a chair.  'yes, yes, Miss,

you need not think to deceive I; I see you from Miss Mercy's window--'



'saw what?' faintly exclaim Charlotte.



'you know well enough,' be the return.  'you may think to blind Mrs.

Beckett here, but I know what over good-nature to young girl come to.

pretty use to make of your fine scholarship, to be encourage

follower and sweetheart, at that time in the morning too!'



'speak up, Charlotte,' say the other occupant of the room, a pleasant

little brisk woman, with soft brown, eye, a clear pale skin, and a

face smooth, in spite of nearly sixty year; 'speak up, and tell Mrs.

Martha the truth, that you never encourage no one.'



the girl's face be all one flame, but she rise up, and clasp she

hand together, exclaimed--'Me encourage!  I never think of what Mrs.

Martha say!  I don't know what it be all about!'



'here, Jane Beckett,' cry Mrs. Martha; 'd'ye see what 'tis to

vindicate she!  will you take her word against mine, that she's be

gossip this half hour with that young rogue as be turn off at

Ormersfield?'



'Tom Madison!  cry the girl, in utter amaze.  'oh! Mrs. Martha!'



'well! I cann't stop!' say Martha. 'I must get Miss Faithfull's

breakfast! but if you be under I, Miss Charlotte, I can tell you it

would be well for you!  you'll sup sorrow yet, and you'll both

recollect my advice, both of you.'



Wherewith the Cassandra depart, and Charlotte, throw her apron

over her face, begin to cry and sob piteously.



'my dear! what be it now? exclaim her kind companion, pull down

her apron, and try to draw down first one, then the other of the

arm which persist in veil the crimson face.  'surely you don't

think missus or I would mistrust you, or think you'd take up with the

like of he!'



'how could she be so cruel--so spiteful,' sob Charlotte, 'when he

only come to ask one question, and do a good turn for I with the

mat.  I never think of such a thing.  Sweetheart, indeed!  so cruel

of she!'



'bless I!' say Jane, 'girl use to think it only civility to say

they have a sweetheart!'



'don't, Mrs. Beckett!  I hate the word!  I don't want no such thing! I

willn't never speak to Tom Madison again, if such construction be to be

put on it!'



'well, after all, Charlotte dear, that will be the safe way. you be

young yet, and well not to think of settle, special if you ben't

sure of one that be steady and religious, and you'd well keep

yourself up, and not get a name for gossip--though there's no harm

do yet, so don't make such a work.  bless I, if I don't hear his

lordship's voice!  he ben't never come so early!'



'yes, he be,' say Charlotte, recover from her sob; 'he ride up as

I come in.'



'well, to be sure, he be come to breakfast!  I hope nothin's amiss with

my young Lord!  I must run up with a cup and plate, and you, make the

place tidy, in case Mr. Poynings come in.  you'd well run into the

scullery and wash your face; 'tis all tear!  you're a terrible one to

cry, Charlotte!' with a kind, cheer smile and caress.



Mrs. Beckett bustle off, leave Charlotte to restore herself to the

little handy piece of household mechanism which kind, patient, motherly

training have render she.



Charlotte Arnold have be fairly educate at a village school, and

tenderly bring up at home till leave an orphan, when she have be

take into her present place.  she have much native refinement and

imagination, which, half cultivate, produce a curious mixture of

romance and simplicity.  her insatiable taste for reading be

meritorious in the eye of Mrs. Beckett, who, unlearn herself,

think any book well than 'gad about,' and, after hear she

daily portion of the Bible, listen to the most adventurous romance,

with a sense of pleasure and duty in keep the girl to her book.

she love the little fragile orphan, teach she, and have patience with

she, and trust the true high sound principle which she recognise in

Charlotte, amid much that she could not fathom, and set down

alternately to the score of scholarship and youth.



Taste, modesty, and timidity be guard to Charlotte.  a broad stare

be terror to she, and she have many a fictitious horror, as well as

well-found one.  truly she say, she hate the broad word Martha

have use.  one who crave a true knight to be twit with a

sweetheart!  Martha and Tom Madison be almost equally distasteful, as

connect with such a reproach; and the little maiden draw into

herself, promenade her fancy in castle and tournament, keep under

Jane's wing, and be uphold by she as a sensible, prudent girl.









CHAPTER II.



an OLD SCHOOLMISTRESS.



  I praise thee, matron, and thy due

  be praise, heroic praise and true;

  with admiration I behold

  Thy gladness unsubdued and bold.

  Thy look and gesture all present

  the picture of a life well spend;

  our human nature throw away

  its second twilight and look gay.

                             WORDSWORTH.





Unconscious of Charlotte's flight and Tom's affront, the Earl of

Ormersfield ride along Dynevor Terrace--a row of house with handsome

cement front, tragic and comic mask alternate over the downstairs

window, and the centre of the block adorn with a pediment and

colonnade; but there be an air as if something ail the place: the

garden be weedy, the glass door hazy, the cement stain and

scar, and many of the window closed and dark, like eye want

speculation, or with merely the dreary word 'to be let' enliven

their blank gloom.  at the house where Charlotte have vanish, he draw

his rein, and open the gate--not one of the rusty one--he enter

the garden, where all be trim and fresh, the shadow of the house lie

across the sward, and preserve the hoar-frost, which, in the

sunshine, be melt into diamond drop on the linger China rose.



without ring or knock, he pass into a narrow, carpetless vestibule,

unadorne except by a beautiful blue Wedgewood vase, and lay down

hat and whip, mount the bare staircase, long since divest of all

paint or polish.  avoid the door of the principal room, he open

another at the side, and stand in a flood of sunshine, pour in from

the window, which look over all the roof of the town, to the

coppice and moorland of Ormersfield.  on the bright fire sing a

kettle, a white cat purr on the hearth, a canary twitter merrily in

the window, and the light smile on a languish Dresden shepherdess

and her lover on the mantelpiece, and dance on the ceiling, reflect

from a beautifully chase silver cream-jug--an inconsistent companion

for the homely black teapot and willow-pattern plate, though the two

cup of rare indian porcelain be not unworthy of it.  the furniture

be the same mixture of the ordinary and the choice, either worn and

shabby, or such as would suit a virtuoso, but the whole arrange with

taste and care that make the effect bright, pleasant, and comfortable.

Lord Ormersfield stand on the hearth-rug waiting.  his face be that of

one who have learn to wait, more considerate than acute, and bear

the stamp both of toil and suffering, as if grief have take away all

mobility of expression, and leave a stern, thoughtful steadfastness.



presently a lady enter the room.  her hair be white as snow, and she

could not have see less than seventy-seven year; but beauty be not

go from her feature--smile be still on her lip, brightness in

her clear hazel eye, buoyancy in her tread, and alertness and dignity

in her tall, slender, unbent figure.  there be nothing so remarkable

about she as the elasticity as well as sweetness of her whole look and

bearing, as if, while she have something to love, nothing could be

capable of crush she.



'you here!' she exclaim, hold out her hand to her guest.  'you be

come to breakfast.'



'thank you; I wish to see you without interrupt your day's work.

have you many scholar at present?'



'only seven, and three go into school at Easter.  Jem and Clara, wish

I to undertake no more, but I should sorely miss the little fellow. I

wish they may do I as much credit as Sydney Calcott.  he write himself

to tell I of his success.'



'I be glad to hear it.  he be a very promising young man.'



'I tell he I shall come to honour, as the old dame who teach he to

spell.  my scholar may make a Dr. Busby of I in history.'



'I be afraid your preferment will depend chiefly on James and young

Calcott.'



'Nay, Louis tell I that he be go to read wonderfully hard; and if

he choose, he can do more than even Sydney Calcott.'



'if!' say the Earl.



Jane here enter with another cup and plate, and Lord Ormersfield sit

down to the breakfast-table.  after some minute' pause he say, 'have

you hear from Peru?'



'not by this mail.  have you?'



'yes, I have.  Mary be come home.'



'Mary!' she cry, almost spring up--'Mary Ponsonby?  this be good

news--unless,' as she watch his grave face, 'it be her health that

bring she.'



'it be.  she have consult the surgeon of the Libra, a very able man,

who tell she that there be absolute need of good advice and a colder

climate; and Ponsonby have consent to let she and her daughter come

home in the Libra.  I expect they in February.'



'my poor Mary!  but she will get well away from he.  I trust he be

not come!'



'not he,' say Lord Ormersfield.



'dear, dear Mary!  I have scarcely dare to hope to see she again,'

cry the old lady, with tear in her eye.  'I hope she will be

allow to be with we, not keep in London with his sister.  London do

she no good.'



'the very purport of my visit,' say Lord Ormersfield, 'be to ask

whether you could do I the favour to set aside your scholar, and

enable I to receive Mrs. Ponsonby at home.'



'thank you--oh, thank you.  there be nothing I should like well, but

I must consider--'



'Clara would find a companion in the young Mary in the holiday, and

if James would make Fitzjocelyn his charge, it would complete the

obligation.  it would be by far the good arrangement for Mary's

comfort, and it would be the great satisfaction to I to see she

with you at Ormersfield.'



'I believe it would indeed,' say the old lady, more touched than the

outward manner of the Earl seem to warrant.  'I would--you know I

would do my very well that you and Mary should be comfortable

together'--and her voice trembled--'but you see I cannot promise all at

once.  I must see about these little boy.  I must talk to Jem. in

short, you must not be disappointed'--and she put her hand before she

face, try to laugh, but almost overcome.



'Nay, I do not mean to press you,' say Lord Ormersfield, gently; 'but

I think, since James have have the fellowship and Clara have be at

school, that you wish to give up your pupil.'



'so I do,' say the lady, but still not yield absolutely.



'for the rest, I be very anxious that James should accept Fitzjocelyn

as his pupil.  I have always consider their friendship as the good

hope, and other plan have have so little success, that--'



'I'm not go to hear Louis abuse!' she exclaim, gaily.



'yes,' say Lord Ormersfield, with a look nearly approach a smile,

'you be the last person I ought to invite, if I wish to keep your

nephew unspoile.'



'I wish there be any one else to spoil he!'



'for his sake, then, come and make Ormersfield cheerful.  it will be

far well for he.'



'and for you, to see more of Jem,' she add.  'if he be your, what

would you say to such hour?'



the last word be aim at a young man who come briskly into the

room, and as he kiss she, and shake hand with the Earl, answer in

a quick, bright tone, 'shocking, aye.  all owe to sit up till

one!'



'read?' say the Earl.



'read,' he answer, with a sort of laugh satisfaction in dash

aside the approval express in the query, 'but not quite as you

suppose.  see here,' as he hold up maliciously a railway novel.



'I be afraid I know where it come from,' say Lord Ormersfield.



'exactly so,' say James.  'it be Fitzjocelyn's desertion of it that

excite my curiosity.'



'indeed.  I should have think his desertion far too common to excite

any curiosity.'



'by no mean.  he always have a reason.'



'a plausible one.'



'More than plausible,' cry James, excitement sparkle in his vivid

black eye.  'it happen that this be the very book that you would most

rejoice to see distasteful to he--low morality, false principle,

morbid excitement, not a line that ought to please a healthy mind.'--



'yet it have interest enough for you.'



'I be not Fitzjocelyn.'



'you know how to plead for he.'



'I speak simple truth,' bluntly answer James, run his hand

through his black hair, to the ruin of the morning smoothness, so that

it, as well as the whole of his quick, dark countenance seem to have

undergo a change from sunny south to stormy north in the few moment

since his first appearance.



after a short silence, Lord Ormersfield turn to he, say 'I have

be beg a favour of my aunt, and I have another to ask of you,'

and repeat his explanation, beg he to undertake the tutorship of

his son.



'I shall not be at liberty at Easter,' say James, 'I have all but

undertake some man at Oxford.'



'oh, my dear Jem!' exclaim the old lady, 'be that settle beyond

alteration?'



'I'm not go to throw they over.'



'then I shall hope for you at Midsummer,' say the Earl.



'we shall see how thing stand,' he return, ungraciously.



'I shall write to you,' say Lord Ormersfield, still undaunted, and

soon after take his leave.



'cool!' cry James, as soon as he be go.  'to expect you to give up

your school at his beck, to come and keep house for he as long as it

may suit he!'



'Nay, Jem, he know how few boy I have, and that I intend to give

they up.  you don't mean to refuse Louis?' she say, imploringly.



'I shall certainly not take he at Easter.  it would be a mere farce

intend to compensate to we for give up the school, and I'll not

lend myself to it while I can have real work.'



'at Midsummer, then.  you know he will never let Louis spend a long

vacation without a tutor.'



'I hate to be at Ormersfield,' proceed James, vehemently, 'to see

Fitzjocelyn browbeaten and contradict every moment, and myself set up

for a model.  I may steal a horse, while he may not look over the wall!

do you observe the inconsistency?--angry with the poor fellow first

for have the book, and then for not read the whole, while it

become amiable and praiseworthy in I to burn out a candle over it!'



'ah! that be my concern.  I tell he he would sing another note if you

be his son.'



'I'd soon make he!  I would not stand what Louis do.  the more he be

set down and sneer at, the more debonnaire he look, till I could

rave at he for take it so easily.'



'I hope you might have hinder they from fret each other, as they

do so often.'



'I should only be a fresh element of discord, while his lordship will

persist in make I his pattern young man.  it make I hate myself,

especially as Louis be such an unaccountable fellow that he willn't.'



'I be sorry you dislike the plan so much.'



'do you mean that you wish for it, grandmamma? cry he, turn full

round on she with an air of extreme amazement.  'if you do, there's an

end of it; but I think you value nothing more than an independent

home.'



'nor would I give it up on any account,' say she.  'I do not imagine

this could possibly last for more than a few month, or a year at the

utmost.  but you know, dear Jem, I would do nothing you do not like.'



'that's nothing to the purpose,' reply James.  'though it be to be

consider whether Ormersfield be likely to be the good preparation for

Clara's future life.  however, I see you wish it--'



'I confess that I do, for a few month at least, which need interfere

neither with Clara nor with you.  I have not see Lord Ormersfield so

eager for many year, and I should be very sorry to prevent those two

from be comfortably together in the old home--'



'and cann't that be without a chaperon?' exclaim James, laugh.

'why, his lordship be fifty-five, and she cann't be much less.  that be

a good joke.'



'it be not punctilio,' say his grandmother, look distressed.  'it

be needful to be on the safe side with such a man as Mr. Ponsonby. my

fear be that he may send her home with order not to come near we.'



'she use to be always at Ormersfield in the old time.'



'yes, when my sister be alive.  ah! you be too young to know about

those matter then.  the fact be, that thing have come to such a pass

from Mr. Ponsonby's neglect and unkindness, that Lord Ormersfield,

stand in the place of her brother, think it right to interfere.

his mother go to London with he, to bring poor Mary and her little

girl back to Ormersfield, and there they be till my sister's death,

when of course they could not remain.  Mr. Ponsonby have just get his

appointment as british envoy in Peru, and wish she to go with he.

it be much against Lord Ormersfield's advice, but she think it she

duty, poor dear.  I believe he positively hate Lord Ormersfield; and

as if for a parting unkindness, he leave his little girl at school with

order to spend her holiday with his sister, and never to be with we.'



'that account for it!' say James.  'I never know all this! nor why we

be so entirely cut off from Mary Ponsonby.  I wonder what she be now!

she be a droll sturdy child in those day!  we use to call she

Downright Dunstable!  she be almost of the same age as Louis, and a

great deal stouter, and use to fight for he and herself too. have not

she be out in Peru?'



'yes, she go out at seventeen.  I believe she be an infinite comfort

to her mother.'



'Poor Mary!  well, we child live in the middle of a tragedy, and

little suspect it!  by the bye, what relation be the Ponsonbys to

we?'



'Mrs. Ponsonby be my niece.  my dear sister, Mary--'



'married Mr. Raymond--yes, I know!  I'll make the whole lucid; I'll

draw up a pedigree, and Louis shall learn it.'  and with elaborate

neatness he write as follow, fill in the date from the first leaf

of an old Bible, after his grandmother have leave the room.  the task,

lightly undertake, become a mournful one, and as he read over his

performance, his countenance vary from the gentleness of regret to a

look of sarcastic pride, as though he feel that the world have deal

hardly by he, and yet disdain to complain.

'since 1816,' mutter James, as he finish.  'thirty year of

drudgery!  when shall I be able to relieve she?  ha!  O. J. F. Dynevor,

Esquire, if it be you who be come from Peru, you would find a

score to settle!'



he run down stair to assist his grandmother in the latin lesson of

her little school, the usual employment of his vacation.



Catharine Dynevor have begin life with little prospect of spend

nearly half of it as mistress of a school.



her father be the last male of the dynevor of Cheveleigh--a family

mount up to the day of the Pendragons--and she have be make to

take the place of an eld son, inherit the extensive land

property on condition that her name and arm should be assume in case

of her marriage.  her choice be one of the instance in which her

affection have the mastery over her next strong characteristic,

family pride.  she marry a highly-educate and wealthy gentleman, of

good family, but of mercantile connexion, such as her father, if

live, would have disdain.  her married life be, however, perfectly

unclouded, her ample mean give she the power of dispense joy, and

her temperament be so blithe and unselfish that no pleasure ever

pal upon she.  Cheveleigh be a proverb for hospitality, affording

unfailing fete for all age, full of a graceful ease and freedom that

inspire enjoyment.



Mr. Frost Dynevor be a man of refined taste, open-handed even to

extravagance, liberal in all his appointment, and gratify to the

utmost his love of art and decoration, while his charity and generous

action be hearty and lavish enough to satisfy even his warm-hearted

wife.



join with all this be a strong turn for speculation.  when the mind

have once become absorb in earthly vision of wealth and prosperity,

the excitement exercise such a fascination over the sense that the

judgment lose balance.  Bold assumption be take as certainty, and

make the foundation of fresh fabric--the very power of discerning

between fact and possibility depart, and, in mere good-will, man,

honest and honourable at heart, risk their own and their neighbour'

property, and ruin their character and good name, by the very action

most foreign to to their nature, ere it have fall under the strong

delusion.



Mr. Frost Dynevor have the misfortune to live in a country rich in

mineral wealth, and to have a brother-in-law easily guide, and with

more love of figure than power of investigate estimate on a large

scale.  Mines be set on foot, company establish, and building

commence, and the result be only to be parallel by those of the

chalybeate spring discover by Mr. Dynevor at the little town of

Northwold, which be pronounce by his favourite hanger-on to be

destine 'literally to cut the throat of Bath and Cheltenham.'



some town be say to have require the life of a child ere their

foundation could be lay.  many a speculation have swallow a life and

fortune before its time for thrive have come.  Mr. Frost Dynevor and

Lord Ormersfield be the foremost victim to the Cheveleigh iron

foundry and the Northwold bath.  the close of the war bring a

commercial crisis that their company could not stand; and Mr.

Dynevor's death spare he from the sight of the crash, which his

talent and sagacity might possibly have avert.  he have show no

misgiving, but, no soon be he remove from the helm, than the

vessel be find on the brink of destruction.  enormous sum have be

sink without tangible return, and the liability of the company far

surpass anything that they have realize.



Lord Ormersfield be stunned and helpless.  Mrs. Dynevor have but one

idea--namely, to sacrifice everything to clear her husband's name. her

son be mere boy, and the only person who prove himself able to act

or judge be the heir of Ormersfield, then about four-and-twenty, who

come forward with sound judgment and upright dispassionate sense of

justice to cope with the difficulty and clear away the involvement.



he join his father in mortgage land, sacrifice timber, and

reduce the establishment, so as to set the estate in the way of

finally become free, though at the expense of rigid economy and

self-denial.



Cheveleigh could not have be save, even have the heiress not be

willing to yield everything to satisfy the just claim of the

creditor.  she be happy when she hear that it would suffice, and

that no one would be able to accuse her husband of having wrong he.

but for this, she would hardly have submit to retain what her nephew

succeed in securing for she--namely, an income of about 150 pound

per annum, and the row of house call Dynevor Terrace, one of the

building venture at Northwold.  this be the sole dependence with

which she and her son quit the home of their forefather.  'never

mind, mother,' say Henry, kiss she, to prevent the tear from

spring, 'home be wherever we be together!' 'never fear, mother,'

echo Oliver, with knitted brow and clench hand, 'I will win it

back.'



Oliver be a quiet lad, of diligent, methodical habit, and willingly

accept a clerkship in a mercantile house, which owe some obligation

to his father.  at the end of a couple of year he be send to reside

in South America; and his parting word to his mother were--'When you

see I again, Cheveleigh shall be yours.'



'oh, my boy, take care.  remember, 'they that haste to be rich shall

not be innocent.''



that be the last time she have see Oliver.



her great object be to maintain herself independently and to complete

Henry's education as a gentleman.  with this view she take up her abode

in the least eligible of her house at Northwold, and, drop the

aristocratic name which alone remain of her heiress-ship, open a

school for little boy, declare that she be rejoice to recall the

day when Henry and Oliver wear frock and learn to spell. if any

human being could sweeten the Latin Grammar, it be Mrs. Frost, with

the motherliness of a dame, and the refinement of a lady, unfaile

sympathy and buoyant spirit, she love each urchin, and each urchin

love she, till she have become a sort of adopt grandmamma to all

Northwold and the neighbourhood.



Henry go to Oxford.  he gain no scholarship, take no honour, but

he fall neither into debt nor disgrace; he lead a goodnatured easy life,

and make a vast number of friend; and when he be not stay with

they, he and his mother be supremely happy together.  he walk with

she, read to she, sing to she, and play with her pupil.  he have

always be bring up as the heir--pet, humour, and wait on--a

post which he fill with goodhumoure easy grace, and which he

continue to fill in the same manner, though he have no one to wait on

he but his mother, and her faithful servant Jane Beckett.  year

pass on, and they seem perfectly satisfied with their division of

labour,--Mrs. Frost keep school, and Henry play the flute, or shoot

over the Ormersfield property.



if any one remonstrate, Henry be always say to be wait for a

government appointment, which be to be procure by the Ormersfield

interest.  More for the sake of his mother than of himself, the

Ormersfield interest be at length exert, and the appointment be

confer on he.  the immediate consequence be his marriage with the

first pretty girl he meet, poor than himself, and all the Ormersfield

interest fail to make his mother angry with he.



the cholera of 1832 put an end to poor Henry's desultory life.  his

house, in a crowded part of London, be especially doom by the deadly

sickness; and out of the whole family the sole survivor be a little

girl of ten month old, and a boy of seven year, the latter of whom

be with his grandmother at Northwold.



Mrs. Frost be one of the woman of whom affection make unconscious

heroine.  she could never sink, as long as there be aught to need she

love and care; and though Henry have be her darling, the very

knowledge that his orphan have no one but herself to depend on, seem

to brace her energy with fresh life.  they be leave entirely on her

hand, her son Oliver make no offer of assistance. he have rise, so as

to be a prosperous merchant at Lima, and he write with regularity and

dutifulness, but he have never propose come to England, and do not

proffer any aid in the charge of his brother's child.  if she have

expect anything from he, she do not say so; she seldom speak of

he, but never without tenderness, and usually as she 'poor Oliver,'

and she abstain from teach her grandchild either to look to

their rich uncle or to mourn over their lose inheritance.  Cheveleigh

be a winter evening's romance with no one but Jane Beckett; and the

grandmother always answer the child's inquiry by bid they

prove their ancient blood by resolute independence, and by that true

dignity which wealth could neither give nor take away.



of that dignity, Mrs. Frost be a perfect model.  a singular compound

of the gentle and the lofty, of tenderness and independence, she have

never cease to be the Northwold standard of the 'real lady,' too mild

and gracious to be regard as proud and poor, and yet too dignified

for any liberty to be attempt, her only fault, that touch of pride,

so ladylike and refine that it be keep out of sight, and never

offend, and everything else so sweet and win that there be

scarcely a being who do not love, as well as honour she, for the

cheerfulness and resignation that have bear she through her many

trial.  her trustful spirit and warm heart have be an elixir of

youth, and have preserve her freshness and elasticity long after her

sister and brother-in-law at Ormersfield have grow aged and sink into

the grave, and even her nephew be fast verge upon more than middle

age.









CHAPTER III.



LOUIS LE DEBONNAIRE.



  I walk by his garden and see the wild bri,

  the thorn and the thistle grow broad and high.

                                        ISC WATTS.





Ormersfield Park be extensive, range into fine break ground, rocky

and overgrown with brushwood; but it bear the mark of retrenchment;

there be hardly a large timber tree on the estate, enclosure have be

begin and desert, and the deer have be sell off to make room for

farmer' cattle, which graze up to the very front door.



the house be of the stately era of Anne, with a heavy portico and

clumsy pediment on the garden side, all the window of the suite of

room open on a broad stone terrace, whence step descend to the

lawn, neatly keep, but sombre, for want of opening in the surround

evergreen.



it be early March, and a lady wrap in a shawl be seat on the

terrace, enjoy the mild gleam of spring, and the freshness of the

sun-warm air, which awake a smile of welcome as it breathe on she

fade cheek, and her eye gaze on the scene, in fond recognition.



it have be the home of Mrs. Ponsonby's childhood; and the slope of

turf and belt of dark ilex be fraught with many a recollection of

girlish musing, youthful vision, and later, interval of tranquillity

and repose.  after fourteen year spend in South America, how many

thread she have to take up again!  she have be as a sister to her

cousin, Lord Ormersfield, and have share more of his confidence than

any other person during their early year, but afterwards their

intercourse have necessarily be confine to brief and guard letter.

she have find he unchanged in his kindness to herself, and she be the

more lead to ponder on the grave, stern impassiveness of his manner to

other, and to try to understand the tone of mind that it indicate.



she recall he as he have be in his first youth--reserve, sensible,

thoughtful, but with the fire of ambition burn strongly within, and

ever and anon flash forth vividly, repress at once as too

demonstrative, but fill she with enthusiastic admiration. she

remember he calmly and manfully meet the shock of the failure,

that would, he know, fetter and encumber he through life--how

resolutely he have face the difficulty, how unselfishly he have put

himself out of the question, how uprightly he have deal by the

creditor, how considerately by his father and aunt, how wise and

moderate his proceeding have be throughout.  she recollect how she

have share his aspiration, and glory in his consistent and prudent

course, without perceive what sorrow have since teach her-that

ambition be to he what pleasure be to other young man.  what have it

not be to she when that ambition begin to be gratify! when he have

become a lead man in Parliament, and by-and-by held office.



there, a change come over the spirit of her dream; and though she

sigh, she could not but smile at the fair picture that rise before

she, of a young girl of radiant loveliness, her golden curl droop

over her neck, and her eye blue as the starry veronica by the hedge

side, smile in the sunshine.  she think of the glance of proud

delight that her cousin have steal at she, to read in her face, that

his Louisa be more than all he have tell she.  Little be need to

make her love the sweet, caress young creature who have throw she

arm round she, and tell she that she see it be all nonsense to tell

she she be such a good, grave, dreadful cousin Mary!  yet there have

be some few misgiving!  so short an acquaintance!  her cousin too

busy for more than be bewitch by the lovely face!  the Villiers

family, so gay and fashionable!  might not all have be foresee? and

yet, of what use would foresight have be?  the gentleman be deeply

attach, and the lady's family court the match, the distinction he

have win, atone for his encumbered fortune.



other scene arise on her memory--Louisa, a triumphant beauty, live

on the homage she receive, all brilliance, grace, and enjoyment. but

there be a darken background which grow more prominent.  Poor

Louisa have little wisdom by nature, and her education have be solely

direct to enable she to shine in the world, not to render she fit for

the companionship of a man of domestic taste, accustom to the

society of superior woman.  there be nothing to fall back upon,

nothing to make a home, she be listless and weary whenever gaiety

fail she--and he, disappoint and baffled, too unbending to draw she

out, too much occupy to watch over she, yield to her taste, and

let she pursue her favourite enjoyment unchecked.



a time have come when childish vanity and frivolity be verge on

levity and imprudence.  expostulation fall powerless on her

shallowness.  Painful be the remembrance of the deprecate roguish

glance of the beautiful eye, and the coaxing caress with which she

kiss away the lecture, and make promise, only to forget they.  she

be like the soulless Undine, with her reckless gaiety and sweetness,

so loving and childish that there be no be displeased with she, so

innocent and devoid of all art or guile in her wilfulness, that her

fault could hardly bear a harsh name than folly.



again, Mrs. Ponsonby think of the day when she herself have be leave

to stay with her old uncle and aunt.  in this very house while her

husband be absent abroad, when she have assist they to receive the

poor young wife, send home in fail health.  she think of the sad

week, so melancholy in the impossibility of make an impression, or

of lead poor louisa from her frivolity, she recall the sorrow of

hear her build on future scheme of pleasure, the dead blank when

her prattle on they fail, the tedium of deep subject, and yet the

bewitch sweetness overpower all vexation at her exceed

silliness.  though full one-and-twenty year have pass, still the

tear thrill warm into Mrs. Ponsonby's eye at the thought of

Louisa's fond clinging to she, in spite of many an admonition and even

exertion of authority, for she alone dare to control the spoilt

child's self-will; and have far more power than the husband, who seem

to act as a check and restraint, and whose presence render she no

long easy and natural.  one confidence have explain the whole.



'you know, Mary dear, I always be so much afraid of he!  if I have have

my own way, I know who it would have be; but there be mamma and

Anna Maria always say how fortunate I be, and that he would be

Prime Minister, and all the rest.  oh! I be far too young and foolish

for he.  he should have marry a sober body, such as you, Mary!  why

do he not?  she wish she have never tease he by go out so much,

and let people talk nonsense; he have be very kind, and she be

not half good enough for he.  that confession, make to he, would have

be balm for ever; but she have not resolution for the effort, and the

day slide away till the bad fear be fulfil.  Nay, be they the

bad fear?  be there not an unavowed sense that it be safe that

she should die, while innocent of all but wayward folly, than be leave

to peril which she be so little able to resist?



the iron expression of grief on her husband's face have forbid all

sympathy, all attempt at consolation.  he have return at once to his

business in London, there to find that poor Louisa's extravagance have

equal her folly, and that he, whose pride it have be to redeem his

paternal property, be throw back by heavy debt on his own account.

this have be know to Mrs. Ponsonby, but by no word from he; he have

never permit the most distant reference to his wife, and yet, with

inconsistency betray his passionate love, he have order one of the

most beautiful and costly monument that art could execute, for her

grave at Ormersfield, and have send brief but explicit order that,

contrary to all family precedent, his infant should bear no name but

Louis.



on this boy Mrs. Ponsonby have found all her hope of a renewal of

happiness for her cousin; but when she have leave England there have be

little amalgamation between the volatile animate boy, and his grave

unbend father.  she could not conjure up any more comfortable

picture of they than the child uneasily perch on his papa's knee,

look wistfully for a way of escape, and his father with an air of

having lift he up as a duty, without know what to do with he or

to say to he.



at her earnest advice, the little fellow have be place as a boarder

with his great aunt, Mrs. Frost, when his grandmother's death have

deprive he of all that be homelike at Ormersfield, he have be with

she till he be old enough for a public school, and she speak of he as

if he be no less dear to she than her own grandchild; but she be

one who see no fault in those whom she love, and Mrs. Ponsonby have

be render a little anxious by a certain tone of dissatisfaction in

Lord Ormersfield's curt mention of his son, and above all by his cold

manner of announce that this be the day when he would return from

Oxford for the Easter vacation.



could it be that the son be unworthy, or have the father's feeling

be too much chill ever to warm again, and all home affection lose

in the strife of politic?  these have ever since engage he, whether

in or out of office, leave little time for society or for any

domestic pursuit.



her reflection be interrupt by a call of 'Mamma!' and her daughter

come run up the step.  Mary Ponsonby have too wide a face for

beauty, and not slightness enough for symmetry, but nothing could be

more pleasing and trustworthy than the open countenance, the steady,

clear, greenish-brown eye, the kind, sensible mouth, the firm chin,

broad though rather short forehead, and healthy though not

highly-colour cheek; and the voice--full, soft, and cheerful--well

agree with the expression, and always bring gladness and promise of

sympathy.



'see, mamma, what we have find for you.'



'violet!  the very purple one that use to grow on the orchard bank!'



'so they do.  Mary know exactly where to look for they,' say Mrs.

Frost, who have follow she up the step.



'and there be Gervas,' continue Mary; 'so charm to hear of you, that

we have almost bring he to see you.'



Mrs. Ponsonby declare herself so much invigorate by Ormersfield air,

that she would go to see her old friend the gardener.  Mary hurry to

fetch her bonnet, and return while a panegyric be go on upon her

ability as maid-of-all-work, in her mother's difficulty with male

housemaid--black and brown--and washerwoman who ride on horseback in

white satin shoe.  she look as if it be hardly natural that any

one but herself should support her mother, when Mrs. Frost tenderly

draw Mrs. Ponsonby's arm into her own; and it be indeed strange to see

the young lady so frail and break, and the eld so strong,

vigorous, and active; as they move along in the sunshine, pause to

note each spring blossom that border the gravel, and enter the

walled kitchen-garden, where espalier run parallel with the walk,

divide the vegetable from the narrow flower-bed, illuminate by

crocus open the depth of their golden heart to the sunbeam and

the revel bee.   Old Gervas, in a patriarchal red waistcoat,

welcome Mrs. Ponsonby with more warmth than flattery.   bless I,

ma'am, I'm right glad to see you; but how old you be!'



'I must come home to learn how to grow young, Gervas,' say she,

smile; 'I hear Betty be as youthful as my aunt here.'



'ay, ma'am, Betty do fight it out tolerablish,' be the reply to this

compliment.



'why, Gervas, what's all that wilderness?  surely those use to be

strawberry bed.'



'yes, ma'am, the early hautboy; don't ye mind?  my young Lord come

and beg it of I, and, bless the lad, I cann't refuse he nothing.'



'he seem to be no gardener!'



'he say he want to make a Botany Bay sort of garden,' say the old

man; 'and sure enough 'tis a garden of weed he's make of it, and mine

into the bargain!  he have a great big thistle here, and the down blow

right over my bed, thick as snow, so that it be three woman's work to

be a match for the weed; but speak to he of pull it up, ye'd think

'twa the heart out of he.'



'do he ever work here?'



'at first it be nought else; he and that young chap, Madison, always

bring dock and darnel out of the hedge, and plant from the

nursery garden, and bring rockwork, and let water in to make a

swamp.  there's no saying what's in the lad's head!  but, of late, he's

not do much but by time lie on the bank, read or speak

verse out loud to himself, or get young Madison off his work to

listen to he.  once he get I to hear; but, ma'am, 'twa all about

fairy and such like, put an ass's head on an honest body as have

lose his way.  I tell he 'twa no good for he or the boy to read such

stuff, and I'd ha' none of it; but, if he choose to read I some good

book, he'd be welcome--for the candle baint so good as they use, and

I cann't get no spectacle to suit I.'



'and do he read to you?'



'a bit or two, ma'am, if the humour take he.  but he's young, you see,

ma'am.  I'm right glad he'll find you here.  my old woman say he do

want a lady about the place to make he comfortable like.'



'and who be this young Madison?' ask Mrs. Ponsonby, when they have

turn from the old gardener.



'to hear Jem, you would believe that he be the most promising plant

rear for Botany Bay!' say Mrs. Frost.  'he be a boy from that wild

place Marksedge, whom Louis take interest in, and make more familiar

than Jem like, or than, perhaps, be good for he.  it do not answer;

the servant do not like it, and it end in his be send to work

with Smith, the ironmonger.  Poor Louis! he take it sadly to heart, for

he have take great pain with the boy.'



'I like to hear the old name, Louis!'



'I cann't help it,' say Mrs. Frost.  'he must be his old aunt Kitty's

Louis le Debonnaire!  don't you, remember your call he so when he

be a baby?'



'oh yes, it have exactly recall to I the sort of gracious look that

he use to have--half sly, half sweet-and so very pretty!'



'it suit he as well now.  he be the kind of be who must have a pet

name;' and Mrs. Frost, hope he might be already arrive, could hardly

slacken her eager step so as to keep pace with her niece's feeble

movement.  she be disappoint; the carriage have return without

Lord Fitzjocelyn.  his hat and luggage be come, but he himself be

miss.  Mrs. Frost be very uneasy, but his father silence

conjecture by say, that it be his usual way, and he would make his

appearance before the evening.  he would not send to meet another

train, say, that the penalty of irregularity must be bear, and the

horse should not suffer for such freak; and he would fain have be

utterly indifferent, but he be evidently listen to every sound, and

betray his anxiety by the decision with which he check all

expression of his aunt's fear.



there be no arrival all that evening, no explanation in the morning;

and Betty Gervas, whom Mary go to visit in the course of the day,

begin to wonder whether the young Lord could be go for a soldier--the

usual fate of all miss village lad.



Mary be on her way home, through the park, along a path skirt the

top of a woode ravine, a dash rivulet make a pleasant murmur

among the rock below, and glance here and there through the

brushwood that clothe the precipitous bank, when, with a sudden

rustling and crackling, a man leap upon the path with a stone in each

hand.



Mary start, but she do not lose her presence of mind, and her next

glance show she that the apparition be not alarming, and be nearly

as much amazed as herself.  it be a tall slight young man, in a suit

of shepherd's plaid, with a fair face and graceful agile form,

recall the word debonnaire as she have yesterday hear it apply. in

instant conviction that this be the truant, she put out her hand by

the same impulse that light his feature with a smile of welcome, and

the year of separation seemed annihilate as he exclaim, 'my cousin

Mary!' and grasp her hand, add, 'I hope I do not frighten you--'



'oh no; but where do you come from?'



'up a hill perpendicular, like Hotspur,' he reply, in soft low quiet

tone, which be a strange contrast to the word.  'no, see here,' and

part the bush he show some rude step, half nature, half art,

lead between the fern and mountain-ash, and look very inviting.



'how delightful!' cry Mary.



'I be glad you appreciate it,' he exclaim; 'I will finish it off now,

and put a rail.  I do not care to go on when I have lose the poor

fellow who help I, but it save a world of distance.'



'it must be very pretty amongst those beautiful fern!'



'you cann't conceive anything more charming,' he continue, with the

same low distinct utterance, but an earnestness that almost take away

her breath.  'there be nine fern on this bank--that be, if we have

the Scolopendrium Loevigatum, as I be persuade.  do you know anything

of fern?  ah! you come from the land of tree fern.'



'oh!  I be so glad to exchange they for our home flower.  primrose

look so friendly and natural.'



'these rock be perfect nest for they, and they even overhang the

river.  this be the good bit of the stream, so rapid and foam that I

must throw a bridge across for Aunt Catharine.  which would be most

appropriate?  I be weigh it as I come up--a simple stone, or a

rustic performance in wood?'



'I should like stone,' say Mary, amuse by his eagerness.



'a rough druidical stone!  that's it!  the idea of rude negligent

strength accord with such place, and this be a stone country.  I know

the very stone!  do come down and see!'



'to-morrow, if you please,' say Mary.  'Mamma must want I, and--but I

suppose they know of your return at home.'



'no, they don't.  they have learn by experience that the right time be

the one never to expect I.'



Mary's eye be all astonishment, as she say, between wonder and

reproof, 'be that on purpose?'



'adventure be thrust on some people,' be the nonchalant reply, with

shoulder depressed, and a twinkle of the eye, as if he purpose

amazing his auditor.'



'I hope you have have an adventure, for nothing else could justify you,'

say Mary, with some humour, but more gravity.



'only a stray infant-errant, cast on my mercy at the junction station.

Nurse, between eat and gossip leave behind--bell ring--engine

squeak--train start--Fitzjocelyn and infant vis-a-vis.'



'you don't mean a baby?'



'a child of five year old, who soon cease howling, and confide his

history to I.  he have be visit grandmamma in London, and be

go home to Illershall; so I find the good plan would be to leave

the train at the next station, and take he home.'



'oh, that be quite another thing!' exclaim Mary, gratify at be

able to like he.  'could you find his home?'



'yes; he know his name and address too well to be lose or mislay. I

would have come home as soon as I have see he in at the door; but the

whole family rush out on I, and conjure I first to dine and then

to sleep.  they be capital people.  Dobbs be superintendent of the

copper and tin work--a thoroughly right-minded man, with a nice,

ladylike wife, the right sort of sound stuff that old England's heart

be make of.  it be worth anything to have see it!  they do

incalculable good with their work-people.  I see the whole concern.'



he launch into an explanation of the process, produce from his

pocket, paper of the ore, in every stage of manufacture, and twist

they up so carelessly, that they would have become a mass of confusion,

have not Mary undertake the repacking.



as they approach the house, the library window be throw up, and

Mrs. Frost came hurry down with outstretche arm.  she be meet by

her young nephew with an overflow of fond affection, before he look

up and beheld his father stand upright and motionless on the high

resign himself perforce to the explanation, and, with the soften

be--Louis, you remember your cousin.'



she be positively startle; for it be as if his mother's deep blue

tint with gold--the same careless yet deprecate glance, the same

engaging smile that warm her heart to he at once, in spite of an air

which be not that of wisdom.

than your father, I should say you be the same Louis that I leave

fourteen year ago.'



'I fear that be the chief change,' say Lord Ormersfield.



'a boy that would be a boy all his life, like Sir Thomas More's son!'

say Louis, coolly and simply, but with a twinkle in the corner of his

eye, as if he say it on purpose to be provoke; and Mrs. Frost

interpose by ask where the cousin have meet, and whether they have

know each other.



'I know he by what you say yesterday,' say Mary.



'Louis le Debonnaire? ask Mrs. Frost, smile.



'no, Mary; not that name!' he exclaim.  'it be what Jem call I,

when he have nothing more cut to say--'



'Aye, because it be exactly what you look when you know you deserve a

scolding--with your shoulder pull down, and your face make up!' say

his aunt, pat he.



when Mrs. Ponsonby and Mary have leave the room to dress, Louis

exclaim, 'and that be Mrs. Ponsonby!  how ill she do look!  she

very voice have break down, though it still have the sweet sound that I

could never forget!  have she have advice?'



'Dr. Hastings see she in London,' say his father.  'he send she into

the country at once, and think that there be fair hope that complete

rest of spirit may check the disease.'



'will she stay here?' say Louis, eagerly.  'that would be like old

time, and we could make she very comfortable.  I would train those two

pony for her drives--'



'I wish she would remain here,' say his father; 'but she be bent on

become my aunt's tenant.'



'ha!  that be next good!  they could do nothing more commendable. will

they be a windfall for the House Beautiful?'



'no,' say Mrs. Frost.  'they wish to have a house of their own, in

case Mr. Ponsonby should come home, or Miss Ponsonby to stay with they.'



'the respected aunt who bring Mary up!  how long have she be at

Lima?'



'four year.'



'four year!  she have not make use of her opportunity!  Alas for the

illusion dispel!  the spanish walk and mantilla melt away; and

behold! the primitive wide-mouthed body of fourteen year since!'



Mrs. Frost laugh, but it seem to be a serious matter with Lord

Ormersfield. 'if you could appreciate sterling worth,' he say, 'you

would be ashamed to speak of your cousin with such conceited

disrespect.'



all the effect be to make Louis walk quietly out of the room; but his

shoulder and eyebrow make a secret telegraph of amazement to Mrs. Frost.



the new arrival seem to have put the Earl into a state of constant

restless anxiety, subdue and conceal with a high hand, but still

visible to one who know he so intimately as do Mrs. Ponsonby.  she

see that he watch each word and gesture, and study her look to

much like weigh and balance the down of Fitzjocelyn's own

favourite thistle; the profusion, the unsubstantiality, and the

volatility be far too similar; and there be something positively

sad in the solicitous heed take of such utter heedlessness.



the reign idea be the expedition to Illershall, and the excellent

condition of the work-people under his new friend the superintendent.

forgetful that mine be a tender subject, the eager speaker become

certain that copper must exist in the neighbourhood, and what an

employment it would afford to all the country round.  'Marksedge must

be the very place, the soil promise metallic vein, the discovery

would be the utmost boon to the people.  it would lead to industry and

civilization, and counteract all the evil we have bring on they.

Mary, do you remember Marksedge, the place of exile?'



'not that I know of.'



'no; we be too young to understand the iniquity.  in the last

generation, it be not the plan to stone Naboth, but to remove he.

great people could not endure little people; so, by way of kindness,

our whole population of Ormersfield, except a few necessary retainer,

be transport bodily from betwixt the wind and our nobility, locate

on a moor beyond our confine, a generous gift to the poor-rate of

Bletchynden, away from church, away from work, away from

superintendence, away from all amenity of the poor man's life!'



this be one of the improvement to which Mr. Dynevor have prompt the

last Earl; but Louis do not know whom he be cut, as he utter

modulate intonation and polished language, the distinctness and

impressive.



'indeed! be there much distress at Marksedge?' say Mrs. Ponsonby.



'they have gift with our own poor at Christmas,' say Lord

distress by their own fault.'



'what cause have they for gratitude?' exclaim his son.  'for be

'it use to be think a very good thing for the parish,' say Mrs.

people, but we do not see thing in the light in which Louis put it.'



'young man like to find fault with the doing of their elder,' say

Lord Ormersfield.



'nothing can make I regard it otherwise than as a wicked sin!' say

Louis.



'Nay, my dear,' mildly say Aunt Catharine, 'if it be mistaken, I be

sure it be not intentionally cruel.'



'what I call wicked be to sacrifice the welfare of dependent to our

own selfish convenience!  and you would call it cruel too, Aunt

Catharine, if you could hear the poor creature beg as a favour of Mr.

Holdsworth to be bury among their kin, and know how it have prey on

the mind of the dying that they might not lie here among their own

people.'



'change the subject, Fitzjocelyn,' say his father: 'the thing be do,

and cannot be undo.'



'the undoing be my daily thought,' say Louis.  'if I could have try

my plan of weave cordage out of cotton-grass and thistle-down, I

think I could have contrive for they.'



Mary look up, and meet his merry blue eye.  be he say it so

gravely to try whether he could take she in?  'if you could--' she

say, and he go off into a hearty laugh, and finish by say, so

that no one could guess whether it be sport or earnest, 'even take

into account the depredation of the goldfinch, it would be an

admirable speculation, and would confer immeasurable benefit on the

owner of waste land.  I mean to take out a patent when I have

succeed in the spinning.'



'a patent for a donkey,' whisper Aunt Catharine.  he respond with a

deferential bow, and the conversation be change by the Earl; but

copper be still the subject uppermost with Louis, and no soon be

dinner over than he follow the lady to the library, and begin

pile of volume, whence be ring the change on oxide, pyrite, and

carbonate, and octohedron crystal--name which poor Mrs. Frost have

hear but too often.  at last it come to certainty that he have see the

very masse contain ore; he would send one to-morrow to Illershall

to be analyse, and bring his friend Dobbs down to view the spot.



'not in my time,' interpose Lord Ormersfield.  'I would not wish for a

great misfortune than the discovery of a mine on my property.'



'no wonder,' think Mrs. Ponsonby, as she recollect Wheal Salamanca

and Wheal Catharine, and Wheal Dynevor, and all the other wheal that

have wheel away all Cheveleigh and half Ormersfield, till the last

unfortunate wheal fail when the rope break, and there be no fund

to buy a new one.  no wonder Lord Ormersfield tremble when he hear

his son launch out into those easily-ascend conjectural

calculation, freely work sum in his head, so exactly like the old

vexatiously cast on his project.



he make the gesture that Mrs. Frost call debonnaire--read on for five

minute in silence, insist on teach his aunt the cause of the

colour in peacock ore, compare they to a pigeon's neck, and talk

of old Betty Gervas's tame pigeon; whence he proceed to memory of

the day that he and Mary have spend together, and ask which of their

old haunt she have revisit.  have she be into the nursery?



'oh yes! but I wonder you have send the old walnut press into that

lumber-room.'



'be that satire?' say Louis, start and look in her face.



'I don't know what you mean.'



'I have a well right to ask what you mean by stigmatize my

apartment as a lumber-room?'



'it be only what I see from the door,' say Mary, a little confused,

but rally and answer with spirit; 'and I must maintain that, if

you mean the room over the garden entrance, it be very like a

lumber-room.'



'ah, Mary! you have not outgrow the delusion of your sex.  be an

Englishman's house his castle while housemaid maraud over it,

ransack his possession, irritate poor peaceful dust that only

want to be let alone, sweep away cherish cobwebs?'



'oh, if you cherish cobwebs!' say Mary.



'do not the fortune of Scotland hang on a spider's thread?  do not a

cobweb save the life of Mahomet, or Ali, or a mediaeval saint--no

matter which?  be not a spider the solace of the Bastille?  have not I

lain for hour on a summer morning watch the tremulous line of the

beautiful geometrical composition?'



'More shame for you!' say Mary, with a sort of dry humorous bluntness.



'the very answer you would have make in old time,' cry Louis,

delight.  'o Mary, you bring I back the day of my youth!  you never

would see the giant who use to live in that press!'



'I remember our great fall from the top of it.'



'oh yes!' cry Louis;  'Jem Frost have set we up there bolt upright for

sentry, and I see the enemy too soon, when you would not allow that

they be there.  I be go to fire my musket at they; but you use

violence to keep I steady to my duty--pull my hair, do not you?'



'I know you scratch I, and we both roll off together!  I wonder we

be not both kill!'



'that do not trouble Jem!  he pick we up, and order we into arrest

under the bed for breach of discipline.'



'I fear Jem be a martinet,' say Mrs. Frost.



'that he be!  a general form on the model of he who, not content

with assault a demi-lune, have take une lune toute entiere.  we have

a siege of the Fort Bombadero, inaccessible, and with mortar fire

double-hand grenade.  they be dandelion clock, and there be

'I remember the nettle,' say Mary, 'and Jem's drive you to gather

they; you stand with your bare leg in the nettle-bed, when he would

'on duty in the trench.  your sense of duty be exemplary.  I

remember your digging on, like a very Casablanca, all alone, in the

midst of a thunder-storm, because Jem have forget to call you in,

cry all the time with fear of the lightning!'



'you come to help I,' say Mary.  'you came rush out from the

nursery to my rescue!'



'I could not make you stir.  we be take prisoner by a sally from

the nursery.  for once in your life, you be in disgrace!'



'I quite think I ought to mind Jem,' say Mary, 'and never know

whether it be play or earnest.'



'only so could you transgress,' say Louis,--'you who never cry,

except as my amateur Mungo Malagrowther.  Poor Mary! what an amazement

it be to I to find you break your heart over the utmost penalty

of the nursery law, when to I they only afford agreeable occasion

of show that I do not care!  I must have be intolerable till you

and Mrs. Ponsonby take I in hand!'



'I be glad you own your obligation,' say Lord Ormersfield.



'I own myself as much oblige to Mary for make I wise, as to Jem for

make I foolish.'



'it be not the cause of gratitude I should have expect,' say his

father.



'alas! if he and Clara be but here!' sigh Louis.  'I entreat he

in term that might have move a pyramid from its base, but the Frost

be arctic.  an iceberg will move, but he be past all melt!'



'I respect his steadiness of purpose,' say the Earl; 'I know no young

man whom I honour more than James.'



his aunt and his son be look towards each other with glisten

eye of triumph and congratulation, and Mrs. Frost clear her voice to

say that he be make far too much of her Jemmy; a very good boy, to

be sure, but if he say so much of he, the Marys would be disappoint

to see nothing but a little fiery Welshman.









CHAPTER IV.



THISTLE-DOWN.



  lightly soar the thistle-down,

    lightly do it float--,

  lightly seed of care be sown,

    little do we note.

  watch life's thistle bud and blow,

    oh, 'tis pleasant folly;

  but when all life's path they strew,

    then come melancholy.

                  Poetry Past and Present.





Mary Ponsonby have lead a life of change and wandering that have give she

few strong local attachment.  the period she have spend at Ormersfield,

when she be from five to seven year old, have be the most joyous

part of her life, and have give she a strong feeling for the place

where she have live with her mother, and in an atmosphere of affection,

free from the shadow of that skeleton in the house, which have darken

her childhood more than she understand.



the great weakness of Mrs. Ponsonby's life have be she over-hasty

acceptance of a man, whom she do not thoroughly know, because she

delicacy have take alarm at foolish gossip about herself and her

cousin.  it be a folly that have be severely visit.  Irreligious

himself, Mr. Ponsonby dislike his wife's strictness; he resent she

affection for her own family, give way to dissipated habit, and make

her miserable both by violence and neglect.  bear late of this unhappy

marriage, little Mary be his only substantial link to his wife, and he

have never be want in tenderness to she: but many a storm have rage

over the poor child's head; and, though she do not know why the kind

old Countess have come to remove she and her mother, and 'papa' be

still a loved and honoured title, she be fully sensible of the calm

security at Ormersfield.



when Mr. Ponsonby have recall his wife on his appointment at Lima,

Mary have be leave in England for education, under the charge of his

sister in London.  Miss Ponsonby be good and kind, but of narrow

view, think all title people fashionable, and all fashionable

people reprobate, jealous of her sister-in-law's love for her own

family, and, though unable to believe her brother blameless, hold it

as an axiom that married people could not fall out without fault on

both side, and charge a large share of their unhappiness on the

house of Fitzjocelyn.  Principle have prevent she from endeavour to

weaken the little girl's affection to her mother; but it have be her

great object to train she up in habit of sober judgment, and freedom

from all the romance, poetry, and enthusiasm which she fancy have be

injurious to Mrs. Ponsonby.  the soil be of the very kind that she

would have choose.  Mary be intelligent, but with more sense than

fancy, more practical than intellectual, and prefer the homely to

the tasteful.  at school, study and accomplishment be mere task,

her recreation be find in act of kindness to her companion, and she

hope be all fix on the go out to Peru, to be useful to her

father and mother.  at seventeen she go; full of active, housewifely

habit, with a clear head, sound heart, and cramped mind, her spirit

even and cheerful, but not high nor mirthful, after ten year of

evening spend in needlework beside a dry maiden aunt.



nor be the home she find at Lima likely to foster the joyousness of

early girlhood.  Mr. Ponsonby be excessively fond of she; but his

affection to she only marked, by contrast, the gulf between he and her

mother.  there be no long any open misconduct on his part, and Mrs.

Ponsonby be almost tremblingly attentive to his wish; but he be

chill and sarcastic in his manner towards she, and her nervous attack

often betray that she have be make to suffer in private for

difference of opinion.  Health and spirit be break down; and,

though she never utter a word of complaint, the sight of her

suffering be try for a warm-hearted young girl.



Mary's refuge be hearty affection to both parent.  she would not

reason nor notice where filial tact teach she that it be good to be

ignorant; she charge all tracasserie on the peruvian republic, and

set herself simply to ameliorate each vexation as it arise, and divert

attention from it without generalize, even to herself, on the state

of the family.  the english comfort which she bring into the limenian

household be one element of peace; and her brisk, energetic habit

produce an air of ease and pleasantness that do much to make home

agreeable to her father, and remove many care which oppress she

mother.  to she, Mary be all the world-daughter, comforter, friend,

and nurse, unfaile in deed of love or word of cheer, and remove

all sense of dreariness and solitude. and Mary have find her mother

all, and more than all she remember, and admire and love she with a

deep, quiet glow of intense affection.  there be so much call for

Mary's actual exertion of various kind, that there be little

opportunity for cultivate or enlarge her mind by book, though the

scene and circumstance around she could not but take some effect.

still, at twenty-one she be so much what she have be at seventeen--so

staid, sensible, and practical, that Miss Ponsonby gladly pronounce

she not in the least spoilt.



Fain would her aunt have keep both she and her mother as her guest;

but Mrs. Ponsonby have permission to choose whatever residence well

suit she, and feel that Bryanston-square and Miss Ponsonby would be

fatal to her harass spirit.  she yearn after the home and

companion of her youth, and Miss Ponsonby could only look severe, talk

of London doctor, and take Mary aside to warn she against temptation

from fashionable people.



Mary have be look for the fashionable people ever since, and the

first sign of they she have see, be the air and figure of her cousin

Fitzjocelyn.  probably good Aunt Melicent would distrust he; and yet

his odd startling talk, and the arch look of mischief in the corner of

his mouth and eye, have so much likeness to the little Louis of old

time, that she could not look on he as a stranger nor as a formidable

be; but be always recur to the almost monitorial sense of

protection, with which she formerly use to regard he, when she share

his nursery.



her mother have cultivate her love for Ormersfield, and she be charm

by her visit to old haunt, well remember everything. she gladly

recognise the little low-browed church, the dumpy tower, and

grave-yard rise so high that it seem to intend to bury the church

itself, and permit many a view, through the lattice, of the seat,

and the Fitzjocelyn hatchment and monument.



she linger after church on Sunday afternoon with Mrs. Frost to look

at Lady Fitzjocelyn's monument.  it be in the chancel, a recumbent

figure in white marble, as if newly fall asleep, and with the lovely

feature chisel from a cast take after death have fix and ennoble

their beauty.



'it be just like Louis's profile!' say Mrs. Frost, as they come out.



'well,' say Louis, who be near than she be aware, 'I hope at least

no one will make I the occasion of a lion when I be dead.'



'it be very beautiful,' say Mary.



'may be so; but the sentiment be destroy by its having be six

month in the Royal Academy, number 16,136, and by see it down among

the excursion in the Northwold Guide.'



'Louis, my dear, you should not be satirical on this,' say Mrs. Frost.



'I never mean it,' say Louis, 'but I never could love that monument.

it use to oppress I with a sense of have a white marble mother!

and, seriously, it fill up the chancel as if it be its show-room,

accord to our family tradition that the church be dedicate to the

Fitzjocelyns.  living or dead, we have take it all to ourselves.'



'it be a very fair, respectable congregation,' say his aunt.



'exactly so.  that be my complaint.  everything belong to his

lordship be respectable--except his son.'



'take care, Louis; here be Mary look as if she would take you at

your word.'



'Pray, Mary, do they let no one who be not respectable go to church in

Peru?'



'I do not think you would change your congregation for the wretched

crowd of brown beggar,' say Mary.



'would I not?' cry Louis.  'oh! if the analogous class here in

England could but feel that the church be for them!--not drive out

and thrust aside, by our respectability.'



'marksedge to wit!' say a good-humour voice, as Mr. Holdsworth, the

young Vicar, appear at his own wicket, with a hearty greeting. 'I

'I hope to be there literally some day this week,' say Louis.  'will
