I miss go to see after the boy last time I be at home.'



'I fear he have not be go on well, and have be sorry for it ever

since,' say the Vicar.  'his master tell I that he find he very

idle and saucy.'



'People of that sort never know how to speak to a lad,' say Louis. 'it

be their own rating that they ought to blame.'



'not Tom Madison, I know,' say Mr. Holdsworth, laugh.  'but I do

not come out to combat that point, but to inquire after the commission

you kindly undertake.'



'I have bring you such a set of prize!  red rubric, red margin;

and for the apparatus, I have bring a globe with all the mountain in

high relief;--yes, and an admirable physical atla, and a box of

instrument and model for apply mathematic to mechanic.  we might

give evening lecture, and interest the young farmer.'



'pray,' say the Vicar, with a sound of dismay, 'where may the bill be?

I think the limit be two pound eighteen.'



'oh!  I take all that on myself.'



'we shall see,' say Mr. Holdsworth, not gratefully.  'be Origen send

home in time for you to bring?'



'there!' cry Louis, starting, 'Origen be lie on the very chair

where I put he last January.  I will write to Jem Frost to-morrow to

send he to the binder.'



'be it of any use to ask for the music?'



'I assure you, Mr. Holdsworth, I be very sorry.  I'll write at once to

Frost.'



'then I be afraid the parish will not be reform as you promise last

Christmas,' say the Vicar, turning, with a smile, to Mrs. Frost.  'we

be to be civilize by weekly concert in the school.'



'what be you to play, Louis?' say Mrs. Frost, laugh.



'I be to imitate all the bird in the air at once,' say Louis,

begin to chirp like a melee of sparrow, turn it into the croak

of a raven, and break off suddenly with, 'I beg your pardon--I

forget it be Sunday! indeed, Mr. Holdsworth, I can say no more than

that I be a wretch not to remember.  next time I'll write it all down

in the top of my hat, with a pathetic entreaty that if my hat be

steal, the thief shall fulfil the commission, and punctually send in

the bill to the Rev. W. B. Holdsworth!'



'I shall hardly run the risk,' say Mr. Holdsworth, smile, as he

part with they, and disappear within his clip yew hedge.



'poor, ill-use Mr. Holdsworth!' cry Aunt Catharine.



'yes, it be base to forget the binding of that book,' say Louis,

gravely.  'I wish I know what amend to make.'



'you owe amend far more for make a present of a commission.  I use

to do the like, to save myself trouble, till I come down in the world,

and then I find it have be a mere air de grand seigneur.'



'I should not dare to serve you or Jem so; but I think the school be

impersonal, and could receive a favour.'



'it be no favour, unless you clearly define where the commission end

and the gift begin.  Careless benefit oblige no one.'



Fitzjocelyn receive his aunt's scolding very prettily.  his manner to

she be a become mixture of the chivalrous, the filial, and the

playful.  Mary watch it as a new and pretty picture.  all his

confidence, too, seem to be her; but who could help pour out his

heart to the ever-indulgent, sympathize Aunt Catharine?  it be

evidently the great treat to he to have she for his guest, and his

attention to she extend even to the reading a sermon to she in the

evening, to spare her eye; a measure so entirely after Aunt Melicent's

heart, that Mary decide that even she would not think her cousin so

hopelessly fashionable.



Goodnatured he be, without doubt; for as the three lady be sit

down to a sociable morning of work and read aloud, he come in to say

he be go to see after Tom Madison, and to ask if there be any

command for Northwold, with his checked shooting-jacket pocket so

puff out that his aunt begin pat and inquire. 'provision for

the House Beautiful,' he say, as forth come on the one side a long

rough brown yam.  'I see it at a shop in London,' he say, 'and think

the Faithfull sister would like to be remind of their west indian

feast.'  and, 'to make the balance true,' he have in the other pocket a

lambswool shawl of gorgeous dye, with wool to make the like, and the

receipt, in what he call 'female algebra,' the long knitting-pin

under his arm like a riding-whip.  he explain that he think it

would be a winter's work for Miss Salome to imitate it, and that she

would succour half-a-dozen family with the proceed; and Mrs.

Ponsonby be pleased to hear he speak so affectionately of the two old

maiden sister.  they be the niece of an old gentleman to whom the

central and handsome house of Dynevor Terrace have be let.  he have

an annuity which have die with he, and they inherit very little but

the furniture with which they have live on in the same house, in hope

of lodger, and pay rent to Mrs. Frost when they have any.  there be

a close friendship and perfect understanding between she and they, and,

as she truly assure they, full and constant rent could hardly have

do she as much good as their neighbourhood.  Miss Mercy be the

Sister of Charity of all Northwold; Miss Salome, who be confine to

her chair by a complaint in her knee, knit and make fancy-work, the

sale of which furnish fund for her charity.  she be highly

educate, and have a great knowledge of natural history.  Fitzjocelyn

have give their abode the name of the House Beautiful, as be

redolent of the essence of the Pilgrim's progress; and the title be so

fully accept by their friend, that the very postman would soon know

it. he linger, discourse on this topic, while Mary repacke his

parcel, and his aunt give he a message to Jane Beckett, to send the

carpenter to no. 5 before Mary's visit of inspection; but she

prophesy that he would forget; and, in fact, it be no good augury

that he leave the knitting-pin behind he on the table, and Mary be

only just in time to catch he with they at the front door.



'thank you, Mary--you be the universal memory,' he say.  'what rest

you must give my father's methodical spirit!  I see you pile up all

those blackwood of mine this morning, just as he be go to fall

upon they.'



'if you see it, I should have expect you to do it yourself,' say

Mary, in her quaint downright manner.



'never expect I to do what be expect,' answer he.



'do you do that because it be not expect?' say Mary, feel almost

as if he be beyond the pale of reason, as she see he adjust a

plant of groundsel in his cap.



'it be for the dicky-bird at my aunt's.  there's no lack of it at the

Terrace; but it be an old habit, and there always be an illusion that

Ormersfield groundsel be a superior article.'



'I suppose that be why you grow go much.'



'be you a gardener?  some day we will go to work, clear the place, and

separate the botanical from the intrusive!'



'I should like it, of all thing!'



'I'll send the horse round to the stable, and begin at once!' exclaim

'my dear! with the groundsel?'

          'Youth be full of pleasance,

CHAPTER V.



the two minister.



  'the creature's neither one nor t'other.

  I catch the animal last night,

  and view he o'er by candle-light;

  I mark he well, 'twa black as jet.

  you stare, but sir, I've get he yet,

  and can produce he.'  'pray, sir, do;

  'and I'll be swear, that when you've see

  the reptile, you'll pronounce he green.'

  'well, then, at once to end the doubt,'

  reply the man, 'I'll turn he out;

  and when before your eye I've set he,

  if you don't find he black, I'll eat he.'

  he say--then, full before their sight

  produce the beast, and lo! 'twa white!

                                     MERRICK.





Mrs. Ponsonby have see in the tropic bird of brilliant hue, that

even, whilst the gazer pronounce they all one beam tint of gorgeous

purple, would give one flutter, and in another light would flash with

golden green or fiery scarlet.  no less startling and unexpected be

the aspect of Lord Fitzjocelyn, 'every thing by start, and nothing

long;' sometimes absorb in study, sometimes equally ardent over a

childish game; wild about philanthropic plan, and apparently

forget they the instant a cold word have fall on they; attempt

everything, finish nothing; dip into every kind of book, and

forsake it after a cursory glance; ever busy, yet ever idle; full of

desultory knowledge, range through all kind of reading and natural

history, and still more full of talk.  this last be perhaps his most

decide gift.  to any one, of whatever degree, he would talk, he could

hardly have be silent ten minute with any human being, except

Frampton or his father, and whether deep reflection or arrant nonsense

come out of his mouth, seem an even chance, though both alike be in

the same soft low voice, and with the same air of quaint pensive

simplicity.  he be exceedingly provoking, and yet there be no be

provoke with he!



he be so sincere, affectionate, and obliging, that not to love he be

impossible, yet that love only make his fault more annoying, and Mrs.

Ponsonby could well understand his father's perpetual restless anxiety,

for his foible be exactly of the sort most likely to tease such a

man as the Earl, and the most positively unsatisfactory part of his

character be the insouciance that he display when his trifling or

his wild project have give umbrage.  yet, even here, she could not but

feel a hope, such as it be, that the carelessness might be the effect

of want of sympathy and visible affection from his father, whose very

anxiety make he the more unbending; and that, what a bad temper

might have resent, render a good one gaily reckless and unheede.



she often wonder whether she should try to give a hint--but Lord

Ormersfield seem to dread lead to the subject, although on all

else that interest he he come to she as in old time, and seem

greatly refresh and soften by her companionship.



an old friend and former fellow-minister have propose spend a night

and, in his own dignified way, he be solicitous that the household

which would give he ample time, and he hope he would be--' he pause

understand.'  with that he walk off, leave Lord Ormersfield telling

other, and he also think the tenor of Fitzjocelyn's future life

'no, my Lord.'



'bring in the dinner.'



Mary long to fly in search of he, and spare further vexation.  she

the soup be over, when she be aware of a step in the hall, and beside

'I entirely forget the right honourable, and march into the

'a bird, hardly ever see in England.  I spy he in the fir-wood,

'the low specie never show off well to stranger,' say Fitzjocelyn,

beg your pardon--indeed I do not do this on purpose, but don't say

anything about appearance--there's something in I that be sure to

revolt.'



so noiselessly that the moment be unknown, the vacant chair be fill

by a gentleman irreproachably attire, his face glow with exercise,

or with what make he very debonnaire and really silent, dine rapidly

and unobtrusively, and never raise his eye even to his aunt,

probably intend thus to remain all the evening; but presently Sir

rose-coloured pastor?'



Louis raise his eye, and meet a pleasing, sensible face, out

beam his arch look of suppress fun as he answer, 'he be not at

all clerical.  he be otherwise call the rose-colour ouzel or

starle.'



'whence be that other startling name?'



'from his attend flock of sheep, on the same mission as jackdaws

fulfil here--which likewise have an ecclesiastical reputation--



     'a great frequenter of the church.''



fear alike nonsense and ornithology, Lord Ormersfield change the

subject, and Louis subside, but when the gentleman come into the

and commit himself either to his wildly philanthropical or

extravagantly monarchical view, whichever might happen to be in the

ascendant.  however, such shoal be not approach, nor do Louis

ever plunge out of his depth.  the whole of his manner and demeanour

be proof that, in his case, much talk spring from exuberance of

idea, not from self-conceit.



he be equally good in the morning: he have rise early to hunt up some

information which Sir Miles want, and the clearness and readiness

the liking be mutual.  no soon have Sir Miles depart, than Louis

out the heart of people.



Mary smile at this, and tell he that he be talk 'like an old

statesman weary of the world.'



'one may be weary of the world beforehand as well as after,' say he.



'that do not seem worth while,' say Mary.



'no,' he say, 'but one's own immediate look-out may not be flattering,

whatever the next turn may bring;' and he take up the newspaper, and

be oblige if you would make two copy of this letter.'



Louis glance at the end of what he be read, lay the paper down,

and open a blotting-book.



'you have well come into the study, or you will not write correctly.'



'I can write, whatever go on.'



'I particularly wish this to be legible and accurate.  you have begin

too low down.'



Louis take another sheet.



'that pen be not fit to write with.'



'the pen be delusion,' say Louis, try they round, in an easy,

idle way: 'I never could mend a quill!  how be this steel one? refuse

to recognise the purpose of his existence.  Aunt Catherine, do you

still forbid steel pen in your school?  if so, it must be the solitary

instance.  how geese must cackle blessing on the inventor! he should

have a testimonial--a silver inkstand represent the goose that lay

the golden eggs,--and all writing-master should subscribe.  ha! where

do this pen come from?  Mary, be you the bounteous mender!  a

thousand thank.'



if Louis fret his father by loitering and nonsense, his father be

no less try by stand over he with advice and criticism which

'thank you,' say Mary; 'I should like it well afterwards.  do you

Louis make polite demur, but she overrule they and begin.



he stretch himself, take up his time, and skim the remain

as the two copy be complete, Lord Ormersfield return; and Mary,

he meekly take another sheet, console himself, however, by say, 'I

warn you that pain will only make it Miss Fanny.'



'what do you mean?'



as if glad to be instigate, he reply, 'do you never hear of my

signature be mistake by an ingenious person, who address his

regular name for I ever since!  I have the envelope somewhere as a

'you seem to be proud of it!' exclaim his father, nearly out of

'I will endeavour, but the Fates must decide.  I can scrawl, or, with

pain, I can imitate Miss Fanny; but the other alternative only come

in happy moment.'



'do you mean that you cannot write well if you choose?'



'it be like other art--an inspiration.  Dogberry be deep when he say

it come by nature.'



'then make no more attempt.  no.  that schoolgirl's niggle be bad

than the first.'



'Fanny, as I tell you,' say Louis, look vacantly up in resign

despair, yet not without the lurk expression of amusement, 'I will

'no, I thank you.  I will have no more time waste.'



Louis passively move to the window, where he exclaim that he see

'do you ever see anything like that?' cry Lord Ormersfield,

thoroughly move to displeasure.



'there be at least good-humour,' say Mrs. Ponsonby.  'pardon I,

there be almost as much to try his temper as your.'



'he be insensible!'



'I think not.  a word from Aunt Catharine rule he.'



'though you counsel it, Mary, I doubt whether her training have

Mary find herself blunder in her new copy, and retreat with it to

the affection between he and Aunt Catherine be the great blessing

'poor boy!' say his father, forget his letter as he stood

'I have no doubt it be to a certain extent true.  yes, absolutely true.

you do not conceive the influence that mood have on some character

but the mere frame of spirit.  even sense of restraint will often take

'it be strong externally,' she say; 'but I think it be more external

than real, more temperament than character.'



'you be too metaphysical for I, Mary;' and he would fain have smile.

'he will not let I.  if I hope at one moment, I be disappoint the

'never.  his conduct have always be blameless; but every tutor have

'blameless conduct!  how many father would give world to be able to

'there be fault that be the very indication of a manly spirit,'

there be substance in he, and like he the well for have a will

and way of his own.'



'so, I think, have Louis; but it be so often thwart, that it sink

away under the sense of duty and submission.'



'if there be any consistency or reason in his fancy, they would not

give way so easily; but it be all talk, all extravagant notion--here

one day, go the next.  not a spark of ambition!'



'ambition be not so safe a spark that we should wish to see it light.'



'a man must wish to see his son hold his proper station, and aim high!

no one can be satisfied to see he a trifl.'



'I have be try to find out why he trifle.  as far as I can see,

make an admirable country gentleman.'



'a mere farmer, idle away his time in his field.'



'no; do infinite good by example and influence, and come forward

'he come to tell you!  Nay, never say he have no resolution!  such

they do not tell their father?'



'if he be like other youth, I should know how to deal with he. but

'perhaps not; he be too impulsive and inexperienced.  if you be to

'I cannot make he care for the estate.  last winter, when he come of

'I wish I could defend he!  and yet--you will think I unreasonable,

concern, he would have attend more.'



'Umph!'



'I be not sure that it be not his good quality that make he so hard

to deal with.  the want of selfishness and vanity seem to take away two

common spring of action, but I do believe that patience will bring out

something much high when you have find the way to reach it.'



'that I certainly have not, if it be there!'



'to cultivate his sympathy with you,' say Mrs. Ponsonby, hesitating,

and not venture to look into his face.



'Enough, Mary,' he say, hastily.  you say the like to I once before.'



'but,' say Mrs. Ponsonby, firmly, '_here_ there be a foundation to

work on.  there be affection that only need to be draw out to make

you happy, and he--not, perhaps, what you now wish, but well than

you wish.'



his face have become hard as he answer, 'thank you, Mary; you have

always mean the good.  you have always be kind to I, and to all

belong to I.'



her heart ache for the father and son, understand each other so

little, and pain each other so much, and she fear that the Earl's

such soften on his part as could alone, as it seem, prevent Louis

Mary have in the mean time complete her copy, and leave they on the

attempt at a call.  the bush and a project rock cut off her view;

but, in some trepidation, she call out, 'be any one there?'  Little

do she expect the answer--



'it be I--Fitzjocelyn.  Come!--I have have a fall.'



'I'm come--be you hurt?' she cry, as with shake limb she

prepare to begin the descent.



'not that way,' he call; 'it give way--go to the left.'



she be almost disobey; but, recall herself to thought, she

hurry along the top till the bank become practicable, and tear she

Horror-strike, she perceive that a heavy stone have give way and

die here,' he say; as she kneel by he, exclaim, 'oh, Louis!

Louis! what a dreadful fall!'



'it be my fault,' he eagerly interrupt.  'I be glad it have happen

to no one else.'



'and you be terribly hurt! I must go for help! but what can I do for

you?  would you like some water?



'Water!  oh! I have hear it all this time gurgle there!'



she fill his cap, and bathe his face, apparently to his great

relief, and she venture to ask if he have be long there.



'very long!' he say.  'I must have faint after I get the stone off

my foot, so I miss Gervas go by.  I think no one else would come

near.  thank God!'



Mary almost grow sick as she see how dreadfully his left ankle have be

crush by a heavy stone; and her very turn towards it make he

'I be afraid I should only hurt you,' she say, with difficulty

control herself.  'I have well fetch some one.'



he do not know how to be leave again; but the damp chilliness of his

she could utter nothing but 'Louis!'



'another accident!' be the almost impatient answer.



'he be dreadfully hurt!' she say, sob and breathless.  'his foot

be crush!  he have be there this hour!'



the alarm be indeed give.  the Earl seem about to rush away without

know whither; and she have absolutely to withhold he, while,

summon her faculty, she give direction to Poynings. then she let

he draw she on, too fast for speak, until they reach the spot

where Louis lay, so spend with pain and cold, that he barely open his

eye at their voice, make no distinct answer as to his hurt, and

shrank and moan when his father would have raise he.



Mary contrive to place his head on her lap, bathe his forehead and

chafe his hand, while Lord Ormersfield stand watch he with look

of misery, or pace about, anxiously look for the servant.



they come at last, all too soon for poor Louis, who suffer terribly

in the transport, and give few token of consciousness, except a cry

now and then extort by a rougher movement.



none of the household, scarcely even Mrs. Frost, seem at first to be

able to believe that Lord Fitzjocelyn could really have hurt himself

seriously.  'again!' be the first word of every one, for his many

slight accident be treat like cry 'Wolf;' but Frampton himself

look perfectly pale and shock when he perceive how the matter

really stand; and neither he nor Lord Ormersfield be half so helpful

as Mrs. Frost.  the shock only call out her energy in behalf of she

darling, and, tender as her nature be, she shrink from nothing that

could soothe and alleviate his suffering; and it do infinitely comfort

he, as he hold her hand and look with affection into her face, even

in the extremity of pain.



Fain would other have be the same support; but his father, though

not leave he, be completely unnerved, and unable to do anything;

and Mrs. Ponsonby be suffer under one of the attack that be

bring on by any sudden agitation.  Mary, though giddy and throb

in every pulse, be force to put a resolute check on herself--brace

her limb, steady her voice, and keep her face compose, while every

faculty be absorb in listen for sound from her cousin's room,

and her heart be quiver with an anguish of prayer and suspense.

could she but hide her burn cheek for one moment, let out one of

the sob that seem to be rend her breast, throw herself on her

knee and burst into tear, what an infinite relief it would be!  but

Mary have learn to spend her life in have no self.









CHAPTER VI.



farewell.



  what yet be there that I should do,

    linger in this darksome vale?

  proud and mighty, fair to view,

    be our scheme, and yet they fail,

  like the sand before the wind,

    that no power of man can bind.

                      ARNDT, Lyra Germanica.





Dynevor Terrace be say to have dark, damp kitchen, but by none who

have ever be in no. 5, when the little compact fire be compress to

one glow red crater of cinder, their smile laugh ruddily back

from the bright array on the dresser, the drugget lay down, the round

oaken table bring forward, and Jane Beckett, in afternoon trim,

tend her geranium, the offspring of the parting Cheveleigh nosegay,

or gauffree her mistress's cap.  no wonder that on raw evening,

Master James, Miss Clara, or my young Lord, have often be find

gossip with Jane, toast their own cheek as well as the bread, or

pinch their finger in her gauffreing machine.



yet, poor little Charlotte Arnold learn that the kitchen could be

dreary, when Mrs. Beckett have be summon to nurse Lord Fitzjocelyn,

and she remain in sole charge, under Mrs. Martha's occasional

supervision.  she find herself, her household care over all too soon,

on a cold light March afternoon, with the clock tick loud enough for

midnight, the smoke-jack indulge in supernatural groan, and the

whole lonely house full of undefined terror, with an unlimited space

of the like solitude before she.  she would even have be glad to be

sure of an evening of Mrs. Martha's good advice, and of darn

stocking!  she sit down by the round table to Mr. James's wristband;

but every creak or crack of the furniture make she start, and think of

death-watch.  she might have learn to contemn superstition, but that

do not prevent it from affect her nerve.



she spread her favourite study, the Old English Baron, on the table

before she; but the hero have some connexion in her mind with Tom

Madison, for whom she have always covet a battle-field in France. what

would he feel when he hear how he have fill up his course of evil,

be well-nigh the death of his benefactor!  if any one ought to be

haunted, it would assuredly be no other than Tom!



Chills run over she at the thought, she turn to the fire as the

thing near life, but at the moment start at a hollow call of her

own name.  a face be look in at she through the geranium!  she

shriek aloud, and clasp her hand over her eye.



'don't make a row.  open the door!'



it be such a relief to hear something unghostly, that she spring to

the door; but as she undo it, all her scruple seize she, and she

try to hold it, say, 'don't come in!  you unfortunate boy, do you

know what you have do?'



but Tom Madison be in a mood to which her female nature cower.  he

push the door open, say authoritatively, 'tell I how he be!'



'he be as ill as he can be to be alive,' say Charlotte, actuate at

once by the importance of be the repository of such tiding, and by

the excitement of communicate they to one so deeply concerned. 'Mr.

Poynings come in to fetch Mrs. Beckett--he would have no one else to

nurse he--and he say the old Lord and Missus have never have their

clothe off these two night.'



'then, be it along of they stone?' ask the lad, hoarsely.



'yourself should know well!' return Charlotte.  'Mr. Poynings say

'twas a piece of rock as big as that warming-pan as crush his ankle!

and you know--'



'I know nothing,' say Tom.  'Master keep I in all day yesterday, and

I only hear just now at Little Northwold, where I've be to take home

some knife of Squire Calcott's.  Master may blow I up if he like,

but I couldn't come till I'd hear the right of it.  be he so very

bad?'



'they've send up to London for a doctor,' pursue Charlotte.  'Mr.

Walby don't give but little hope of he.  poor young gentleman, I'm

sure he have a good word from high and low!'



'well! I'm go!' cry Tom, vehemently.  'goodbye to you, Charlotte

Arnold!  you'll never see I in these part more!'



'Gone!  oh, Tom! what do you mean!'



'D'ye think I'll stay here to have this here cast in my face?  such a

one as willn't never walk the earth again!' and he burst out into

passionate tear.  'I wish I be dead!'



'oh, hush, Tom!--that be wicked!'



'may be so!  I be all that's wicked, and you all turn against I!'



'I don't turn against you,' sob Charlotte, move to the bottom of

her gentle heart.



'you! you turn against I long ago.  you've be too proud to cast

one look at I these three month; and he forget I; and that's what

draw I on, when who care what become of I--nor I neither now.'



'don't speak that way!  don't say 'twas pride.  oh no! but I have to

behave proper, and how should I keep up acquaintance when they say you

go on--unsteady--'



'aye, aye!  I know how it be,' say poor Tom, with break-down

humility: 'I be not fit for you then, and I'm next thing to a murderer

now; and you're like a white dove that the very finger of I would

grime.  I'll take myself out of your way; but, let what will come of

I, I'll never forget you, Charlotte.'



'oh, wait, Tom!  if I could but say it right!--Oh! I know there's

something about bide patiently, and get a blessing--if you'd only

stop while I recollect it.'



'I think I hear voice!' exclaim Mrs. Martha, suddenly descend

on they.  'I wonder you ben't ashamed of yourself, and the family in

such trouble!  downright owdacious!'



'be this your house?' say Tom, step before Charlotte, his

dejection give way instantly to rude independence.



'oh, very well,' say Martha, with dignity.  'I know what to expect

from such sort of people.  the house and young woman be in my charge,

sir; and if you don't be off, I'll call the police.'



'never trouble your old bone!' retort Tom.



'good-bye to you, Charlotte;' and, as in defiance of Martha, he take

her passive hand.  'you'll remember one as love you true and faithful,

but be drive desperate!  good-bye!  I'll not trouble no one no more!'



the three concluding negative with which he dash out of the house

utterly overwhelmed Charlotte, and make she perfectly insensible to

Mrs. Martha's objurgation.  she believe in the most horrible and

desperate intention, and sob herself into such violent hysteric

that Miss Mercy come in to assist--imagine that the rude boy have

terrify she, misunderstood her shameface attempt at explanation,

and leave she lie on her bed, cry quietly over her secret terror,

and over that first, strangely-make declaration of love. the white

dove! she do not deserve it, but it be so poetical! and poor Tom be

so unhappy!  she have not time even to think what be become of her own

character for wisdom and prudence.



the next morning, between monition and triumph, Martha announce that

the good-for-nothing chap be off with a valuable parcel of Mr.

Calcott's, and the police be after he; with much more about his

former idle habits,--frequenting of democratic oratory, public-house,

and fondness for bad company and stroll actor.  Meek and easily

cow, Charlotte only open her lip to say she know that he have take

home Mr. Calcott's parcel.  but this bring down a storm on she for

be impertinent enough to defend he, and she sat tremble till it

have subside; and Martha retreating, leave she to weep unrestrainedly

over her wild fancy, and the world's cruelty and injustice towards

one whom, as she be now ready to declare, she love with her whole

heart.



the bell ring sharply, knock rattle at the front door!  she be sure

that Tom have be just take out of the river!  but instinct to answer

the bell awake at the second furious clattering and double pealing,

which allow no time for she to compose her tear-streak, swollen

face, especially as the hasty sound suggest 'Mr. James.'



Mr. James it be, but the expect rebuke for keep he wait be

not speak.  as he see her sorrowful look, he only say, low and

softly, 'be it so, Charlotte?'  in his eye, there could be but one

cause for grief, and Charlotte's heart smite she for hypocrisy, when

she could barely command her voice to reply, 'no, sir; my Lord have have

a little well night.'



he speak with unusual gentleness, as he make more inquiry than she

could answer; and when, after a few minute, he turn to walk on to

Ormersfield, he say, kindly, 'good-bye, Charlotte; I'll send you word

if I find he well:' and the tear rise in his eye at the thought

how every one love the patient.



he be not wrong.  there be everywhere great affection and sympathy

for the bright, fantastic be whom all laugh at and like, and

Northwold and the neighbourhood feel that they could have well spare

something more valuable.



the danger be hardly exaggerate even by Charlotte.  the chill of the

long exposure have bring on high fever; and besides the crushed ankle,

there have be severe contusion, which have result in an acute pain

in the side, hitherto untouched by remedy, and beyond the

comprehension of the old Northwold surgeon, Mr. Walby.  as yet,

however, the idea of peril have not present itself to Louis, though he

be perfectly sensible.  severe pain and illness be new to he; and

though not fretful nor impatient, he have not the stoicism either of

pride or of physical indifference, put little restraint on the

expression of suffering, and be to an almost childish degree absorb

in the present.  he be always considerate and grateful; and his fond

affection for his Aunt Catharine, and for good old Jane, never fail

to show itself whenever they do anything for his relief; and they be

the good of nurse.



Poor Lord Ormersfield long to be equally effective; but he be

neither handy nor ready, and could only sit hour after hour beside his

'no harm! lurk here in that fashion in the dark!  you'll not make I

CHAPTER VII.



GOSSAMER.



  Chaos be come again.--Othello.





that sleep be not unto death.  when James and Mary come simultaneously

Mary read.





'DEAREST JEMMY,--Write to I as quick as ever you can, and tell I how

Louis be; and let I come home, or I shall run mad.  it be no good

tell I to command my feeling; I be sure I would if I could, for

the girl be more detestable than ever; but what can one do when one

cannot sleep nor eat?  all the screaming and crying have get into one

bump in my throat, because I cann't get it out in peace.  if I could

only shy the inkstand at the English teacher's head! or get one moment

alone and out of sight!  let I come home.  I could at least run

message; and it be of no use for I to stay here, for I cann't learn,

and all the girl be look at I.  if they be but boy, they would

have sense! or if I could but kick they!  this will make you angry, but

do forgive I; I cann't help it, for I be so very unhappy.  Louis be as

much to I as you be, and no one ever be so kind; but I know he will

get well--I know he will; only if I know the pain be well, and could

but hear every minute.  you need not come to fetch I; only send I a

telegraph, and one to Miss Brigham.  I have money enough for a

second-class ticket, and would come that instant.  if you see the eye

and hear the whisper of these girl, I be sure you would.  I should

laugh at such nonsense any other time, but now I only ask to be

wretche quietly in a corner.



              'your affectionate, nearly crazy, sister,

                                       'CLARA FROST DYNEVOR.'





Mary might well say that there be nothing more expedient than go to

'this be Richardson's veto, two degree bad than Frampton's; and I

the Earl give the little worn volume, say, 'yes, Louis, there have

     'I be come that they might have life,

      and that they might have it more abundantly.''



the Bible sink in his hand, and he fall into a slumber so sound and

     'I be come that they might have life,

      and that they might have it more abundantly.'



the word return on he, and with they what his father have say, 'you

CHAPTER VIII.

  gather up each break thread.

                             WHYTEHEAD.





'Tom Madison be come back,' say the Vicar, as he sit beside

Fitzjocelyn's couch, a day or two after Lord Ormersfield have go to

London.



'come back--where have he be?' exclaim Louis.



'there!' say the Vicar, with a gesture of dismay; 'I forget that you

be to hear nothing of it!  however, I should think you be well

enough to support the communication.'



'what be it?' cry Louis, the blood rush into his cheek so

'I wish there be anything more gratifying to tell you; but he take

the opportunity of the height of your illness to run away from his

pain, it be very mortifying, but--'



'Pains!  don't you know how I neglect he latterly!' say Louis.

'poor fellow--then--' but he stop himself again, and add, 'you

hear nothing of the ground?'



'they be not difficult to find,' say Mr. Holdsworth.  'it be the old

disposed to be saucy and disobedient, take up with the most

good-for-nothing boy in the town, haunt those Chartist lecture,

and never come home in proper time at night.  the very last evening,

he have come in at eleven o'clock, and when his master rebuke he, come

out with something about the right of man.  he be send to Little

Northwold, about the middle of the day, to carry home some

decline prosecute for run away, be only too glad of the

'if you feel so,' say Mr. Holdsworth, hope to lead he from the

unfortunate subject, 'what must _we_ do?'



'my position, if I live, seem to have as much power for evil, without

the supernatural power for good.  do hastily, or leave undone, be

equally fatal!'



'Nay, what hope can there be but in fear, and sense of responsibility?'



'I think not.  I do more mischief than those who do not go out of their

'do you!' say the Vicar, smile.  'at least, I know, for my own part,

I prefer all the trouble and perplexity you give I, to a squire who

'the sight of self indulgence spoil more than injudicious care do.

besides, I look on these experiment as give experience.'



'nice experience of my good effort!'



'pardon I, Fitzjocelyn, have we see your good?'



'I hope you will!' say Louis, vigorously.  'and to begin, will you

Mr. Holdsworth have an unmitigated sense of his own indiscretion, and

not such a high one of Fitzjocelyn's discretion as to make he think

accordingly, in due time, Mr. Holdsworth admit the lad, and, on a

the lad look up, but apparently see little such assurance in the thin

'I couldn't stay!' burst out Tom, half cry.



'why not?'



'not to have that there cast in my tooth!' he exclaim, with blunt

incivility.



'do any one reproach you?' say Louis, anxiously.  'I think no one

know it but ourselves.'



'you know it, then, my Lord?' ask Tom, stare.



'I find out directly that there be no cement,' say Louis.  'I have

my Lord.  and you never say nothing, my Lord!'

quite as much my fault as your.  that would not have be fair play.'



'if I willn't give it to Bill Bettesworth!' cry Tom.



'what have he done?'



'always tell I that gentlefolk haven't get no notion of fair play

there--I'll let he know--'

'a young man what work with Mr. Smith,' return Tom, his sullenness

would have deem too free and ready.

'a chap must speak to some one,' be Tom's answer.



'and what kind of a some one be he?'



'why, he come down illershall way.  he know a thing or two, and can

go on like an orator or a play-book--or like yourself, my Lord.'



'thank you.  I hope the thing or two be of the right sort.'



Tom look sheepish.



'I hear something about bad companion.  I hope he be not one.  I

ought to have come and visit you, Tom; I have be very sorry I do

be bad scrape than this of the stone.'



'bad!' cry Tom--'sure nothing could be worserer!'



'I wish there be no evil bad than careless forgetfulness,' say

'I don't forget!' say Tom.  'I mean to have tell you whenever you

come to see I, but'--his eye fill and his voice begin to

and Bill and the rest of they be always at I, ask when I expect

my aristocrat, and jeer I 'cause I'd say you ben't like the rest

half confession, half vindication ensue, and the poor fellow's story

be manifest enough.  his fault have be unsteadiness and misplace

hear that the police be in pursuit of he, he have fancy it be

paradox musingly utter, and fly over his head, wear to Tom a

'DEAR MR. DOBBS,--You would do I a great kindness by employ this

                            'your very truly,

                                   'fitzjocelyn.'

'some day, when you be a foreman, perhaps I may bring Miss Clara to

and he _be_ a thorough farmer's friend, as they all say,' earnestly

'Mary,' say Mrs. Frost.

at she with a look somewhat perplexed, but brim full of mischievous

'he be go down with Mary to his cottage-building.'

would not know himself!)--should enter on no profession, but forthwith

'you leave Prince Roland in happy ignorance,' say Mrs. Ponsonby,

'certainly--or he would fly off like a sky-rocket at the first symptom

express his contempt for needy nobility, and I be afraid, after all

'very fond.  if anything would triumph over his dislike, it would be

'for shame!  I think well of he.  I believe he would be unworldly

Mary's husband must not lean on she for strength.



she be glad, as with new meaning, she watch their proceeding, to

the field be decidedly open to Roland Dynevor, alia James Frost.

Louis, in high spirit, offer to drive Mrs. Ponsonby to meet the

'see to the luggage!  you willn't let he turn I out!  I must sit

'you must have manner,' say Louis; 'look round, and speak rationally

'I never see she be there!' and slightly colouring, the 'Giraffe'

'madcap!' he say; 'but I suppose the first day of the holiday must be

so they drive off, James sit by Mrs. Ponsonby, and take care to

gain; 'be you able to walk?'



'after a fashion.'



'and do your ankle hurt you?'



'only if I work it too hard.  one would think that lounging have become

'don't say a word:' he lower his voice.  'ride be against order,

but I cannot miss the Yeomanry, under the present aspect of affair.'

'you be quite right,' cry Clara.  'you ought to take your vassal,

'exactly so.  our volunteer force be our strength and glory, and be

'dear granny!  but I wish we be go home to the Terrace.'



'thank you.  how flattering!'



'you would be always in and out, and it would be so much more

comfortable.  be Lord Ormersfield at home?'



'no, he will not come till legislation can bear London no long.'



'Oh!'--with a sound of great relief.



'you don't know how kind he have be,' say Louis, eagerly.  'you will

Clara laugh, but sigh.  'I think we should have have more fun at

'what! than with I for your host?  try what I can do.  besides, you

'but she have be at school!'



'well!'



'I don't bargain for school-girl at home!'



'I should not have class Mary in that category.'



'don't ask I to endure any one who have be at school!  oh, Louis! if

'Aunt Kitty will not consent, I be sure, if you be really unhappy

there, my poor Clara.'

I may be able to keep it in!'



out pour the whole flood of trouble, unequal in magnitude, but most

be vulgar.



'I be sure I want to walk with none of they,' say Clara, 'and when

Salter be so very particular.'



'I should like to have hear your contradiction.'

mean to do the same, for nothing would ever make I have anything to

'that spoil it,' say Louis--'the first half be dignified.'



'what be the second?'



'human nature,' say Louis.



'I see,' say Clara.  'well, they be famously scandalize, and that

'I!'



'ay!  'twas my own fault, though, but I couldn't help it.  you must

'of course; she have compassion on your distress--amiable feeling!'

'my dear Giraffe, bend down your neck, and don't take such a

'then take the other side--see the good.'

'by no mean.  I always make the bad of Frampton, and now I know what

'I should like to know what good you would make of these girl!'



'you have to try that!'



'can I get any possible good by stay?'



'a vast deal.'



'I'm sure italian, and music, and drawing, be not a good compare with

'all those thing only grow by stay wherever we may happen to be,

'tell I what good you mean!'



'learn not to hate, learn to mend your glove.  don't jerk the

Clara could extract no more, nor do she wish it, for having relieve

'Mary be to say nothing,' say Louis, 'I mean that poor child to have

'query as to the benefit of that recollection.'

'that I never see evil lessen by private self-exaltation.'

'Pshaw!  primary motive be understand, secondary stand common wear

'as long as they don't eat into the primary.'



'the long and short of it be,' exclaim James, impatiently, 'that we

Clara go into a choke paroxysm of laughter, which her brother try

to moderate by a look, and Louis render more convulsive by quote



     'mark you his cheek of heavenly blue,'



and look with a mischievous amusement at James's ill-suppress

displeasure at the merriment that know no bound, till even Mrs. Frost,

'take care, Aunt Kitty,' say Louis; 'you'll make I restive.  a tutor

'briton never shall be slave!' be the eager response.



'Worthy of the daughter of the Pendragons,' say Louis; 'but it lose

'briton never shall be slave!'  Liberty, fraternity, and equality!

tyrant, beware!'



'you ungrateful boy!' say Mrs. Frost; 'that's the way you use your

'only the way the nineteenth century treat all its good governess,'

'when it get past they,' say Mary, smile.  'I hope you do not

Mary find the renunciation more complete than perhaps she have

'take my advice, Mary, let she alone.  follow your own judgment, and

I always wish it, and I'm glad it have come before they have make she

will be soon enough, without tease she.'



Mary wish to ask whether all this be for Clara's good, but she

the cousin, the one fiery and sensitive, the other cool and impassive,

and yet as appropriate to each other as the pepper and the cucumber, to

borrow a bon mot from their neighbour, Sydney Calcott.

constantly do this occur, that Aunt Kitty, in her love of mischief,

whisper to Mrs. Ponsonby that she only hope the two vizier would

not quarrel about the three thousand sequin, three land estate, and

three slave.



still, Louis's desertion have leave unoccupied so many of the hour of

Mary's time that he have previously absorb, that her mother watch

as a matter of course.  she have attend to her cousin when he need

she, and now that he have regain his former companion, Clara, she

resign he without effort or mortification, as far as could be see.

she be force to fall back on other duty, furnish the house,

work for every one, and read some book that Louis have bring

before she.  the impulse of self-improvement have not expire with his

attention, and without any shadow of pique she be always ready to play

the friend and eld sister whenever he need she, and to be grateful

Ormersfield's return cause Clara's noise to subside so entirely, that

her brother be sufficiently at ease to be exceedingly vivacious and

entertaining, and Mrs. Ponsonby hope for a great improvement in the

state of affair.









CHAPTER X.



the better part of VALOUR.



  for who be he, whose chin be but enrich

  with one appear hair, that will not follow

  these culled and choice-draw cavalier 'gainst France?

  Work, work your thought, and therein see a siege.

                                         King Henry V.





the next forenoon, Mary meet James in the park, wander in search of

his pupil, whom he have not see since they have finish their morning's

work in the study.  some wild freak with Clara be apprehend, but

and clank meet her ear.

be capable of anything!  why do I not think of it soon?'

voice before she, 'can you see they?'



'Scarcely,' say Mary, make room for she.



'where's Louis'!' hastily demand her brother.

'exertion in a good cause can do no harm!' boldly declare Clara;

then, with sudden loss of confidence, 'do you really think it will?'



'just cripple he for life,' say James.



'Mr. Walby wish he not to attempt riding,' say Mary.  'he think

anything for the good of his country.'



'don't say another word, Clara,' exclaim James, 'or you will drive I

Clara have another milder and more sorrowful version of the scolding

and do not miss his son.



it be an exceedingly sultry day, and the hopeful trust that Louis

'I hope you be satisfied,' he say, 'dulce est pro patria mori.'



Louis be slowly dismount, and as he touch the ground give a

'Fitzjocelyn--! what imprudence!'



'take care,' hastily interrupt James, find Louis lean

they lift he up the step into the hall, where he sign to be lay

he drag himself along with more difficulty than he have find for

'ha, Clara!' say Louis, presently; 'you look as if you have be the

'I don't care,' exclaim Clara,  'I know you be in the good old

'oh, Louis! but at least you be in your place--you set the example.'



'unluckily, these thing descend from the sublime to the other thing,

be cross to yourself.'

these weigh upon his mind so much, that he go on, half aloud,

he be past go in to dinner, and the party be 'civil and

his silent mortification and kind anxiety go to his son's heart.

that have be James's first dictum, and he adopt it from conviction.

for himself nor anybody else.









CHAPTER XI.



a HALTING proposal.



  Shallow.  will you upon good dowry, marry she?

  Slender.  I will do a great thing than that, upon your request.

                                  Merry Wives of Windsor.





the first thing that Louis do appear to care for be a letter that

arrive about three day previous to their departure, address to

'Lord Fitsgosling, Hawmsfield Park, Northwold.'  rather too personal,

as he observe, he must tell his correspondent that it hurt his

feeling.  the correspondent be Tom Madison, whose orthography lag

lecture on Kemistry.'  his penmanship be much improve, and he be

up at the Teras.'

'so much the more reason against it!  I ought to wish she to forget

'only you must come out of the dew, the sun be quite go.'

'I well believe it!'

'it be what I mean.'



'ah I but how to make that knowledge help we.'



'there's the point.  now and then, I think I see; but then I go off on

and yet, after all, there be a highway; and wayfare man, though

'oh! tell I how to see my highway at school!'



'if I only keep my own at home, I might.  but you have the

'my purpose!' say Clara.  'I suppose that be to learn as fast as I

'that's plain enough,' say Clara: 'to keep my tone from be

'but ought not one to keep out of what be wrong?'

'I'll try, but--'



'but, mind, it take kind office to bring the blossom out.

there--that's pretty well, consider our mutual sentiment as to good

'have you be give I good advice?'



'not bad, I hope.'



'I think only people like--like Mary--could give advice.'



'ah! your blindness about Mary invalidate your opinion of your

'quite wrong.  not only be she full of clear, kind, solid sense, like a

'such a sensible letter, that I be quite ashamed of it,' say Louis.



'I wonder at the time you choose for writing, when you be so soon to

'I have no excuse, if you think it uncivil.  I never have spirit to set

his father begin at once to speak softly: 'no, I intend no blame; I

only cannot but wonder to see you so much engross with Clara Dynevor.'



'poor child! she want some compensation.'



'I have no doubt of your kind intention; but it would be safe to

'I do not mean that you could be deficient in ordinary courtesy; but I

calculated--' Lord Ormersfield for once fail in his period.



'be we talk at cross purpose?' exclaim Fitzjocelyn.  'what have

I be do, or not do?'



'if my meaning require explanation, it be needless to attempt any.-- be

your ankle painful to-night?'



not a word more, except about his health, could Louis extract, and he

whom have he be neglect?  what have his father be hope?  who be

eminently calculate, and for what?



it flash upon he all at once.  'I see! I see!' he cry, and burst

then come consternation, or something very like it.  he do not want to

all this should be suddenly bring down from cloudland, to sink into

yet the notion would not be at once dismiss.  his declaration that he

his heart thrill and glow as he think of give his father real

he be touch by see how his father's well feeling have conquer

and then Mary could always find everything he want!

this last encourage reflection always wound up the series that

burn my cheek, to look anything near her age!'  and he laugh at

himself, but end with a long-draw sigh.



he be in no state of mind to pause: he be tired of self-debate, and

'we will say no more about it,' be the kind answer.  'if you cannot

turn your thought in that direction, there be an end of the matter.'



'I think,' say Louis, 'that I could.'



'my dear boy,' say the Earl, with more eagerness than he could quite

'I believe so,' say Louis, gaze from the window.



'right,' cry the Earl, with more gladness and warmth than his son have

'I know it,' say Louis.  'she be very good.  she make I very happy

when I be ill.'



'you have see she under the most favourable circumstance.  it be the

her clear, sensible eye be raise, and her colour deepen, but the

Poor Louis! be this his romantic and poetical wooing!

'I do not know.  I cannot tell what I ought to say,' she return,

'well--propose what?'



'Myself;' most innocently whisper.

'you be not at all prepare.'

the total absence of jealousy convince Mrs. Ponsonby that the heart

'oh, mamma, you know you could not spare I.'



'if this be the real thing, dearest--'



'no,' whisper Mary, 'I could not leave you alone with papa.'

'oh, mamma!'

neither speak till she renew her first question,



'what be to be do?'



'shall I go and speak to he, my dear?'



'perhaps I have well, if you will come with I.'



then, hesitating--'I will go to my room for a moment, and then I shall

'candidly, father,' say Louis, 'much as I like she, I cannot be sorry

dear, soft-hearted Aunt Kitty, with all her store of comfort ready

she be just wise enough to keep back the wish of the other vizier,

Clara's alert wit perceive that so many private interview have some

but Clara, recover from her first stupor of wonder, begin scold

dear, dear Louis must get over it, and have the most beautiful wife in

the world.  don't you remember, Jem, the lady with the splendid dark

eye on the platform at Euston Square, when you so nearly make we miss

the train, with the brow that you said--'



'Hush, Clara, don't talk nonsense.'









CHAPTER XII.



CHILDE ROLAND.

'no carmine?  nor scarlet lake in powder?'



'could procure some, my Lord.'



'thank you, the actinia would not live.  I must take what I can find. a

lump of gamboge--'



'if you stay much long, he will not retain his sense,' mutter

James Frost, who be lean backwards against the counter, where the

that perplex customer, Lord Fitzjocelyn.



'some drawing-paper.  this be hardly absorbent enough.  if you have any

block sketch-books?--'



'could procure some, my Lord.'



James look at his watch, while the man dive into his innermost

colour--yes; and the Seaside Book.  I'll choose one or two. what be

James begin to whistle; but Louis, take up a volume, became engross

'do you ever see--' begin Jem.



'most striking.  I do not know you have meet with she.  what an

'Elizabeth Barrett.  be she there?'



'be that her name?  do you know she?'

'who?  do not some lady come into the shop?'

you may see she yet, as she come out.  come back!'

and, laugh, he watch Jem hurry up the slope street and turn the

the numerous black collier and lighter show that precaution be

'she be get into the carriage as I turn the corner; so I go

here.'



'depend on it, they be meditate a descent on his lordship.'

be one by one appear, there emerge an old, grizzly,

'thank you, Captain!' cry Fitzjocelyn.  'I've bring home the boat

safe, you see, by my own superhuman exertion--no thank to Mr. Frost,

there!'



'that's his way, Captain,' retort Jem, leap out, and help his

cousin: 'you may thank I for get he home at all!  but for I, he

would have his back against the counter, and his head in a book, this

very moment.'



'ask he what he be after,' return Louis.



'which of we d'ye think most likely to lag, Captain Hannaford?' cry

Jem, prevent the question.



'which would you choose to have on board?'



'Ye'd both of ye make more mischief than work,' say the old seaman,

who have be look from one to the other of the young man, as if they

be perform a comedy for his special diversion.



'so you would not enter we on board the Eliza Priscilla?' cry Louis.



'no, no,' say the old man, shrewdly, and with an air of hold

something back; whereupon they both press he, and obtain for

answer, 'no, no, I wouldn't sail with you'--signing towards

Fitzjocelyn--'in my crew: ye'd be more trouble than ye're worth.  and

as to you, sir, if I wouldn't sail with ye, I'd like still less to sail

under you.'



he finish with a droll, deprecate glance, and Louis laugh

heartily; but James be silent, and as soon as they have enter the

little parlour, declare that it would not do to encourage that old

'an old man's opinion of two youngster be not what I call impudence,'

those two cousin have never spend a happy month than in these small

coast trader, now the charge of his only survive son; for this be

and brow.



'no; I have no such notion.  I only feel that her presence have the

gladdening, inspiriting, calm effect of moonlight or starlight. I

hear the sound of it--that be like it!  I do not think it be such a

confirm case.  I should have go on in peace but for this letter,

and never think about it at all.'



'so much the well for you!'



'my father be too just and candid not to own his error, and be

thankful.'

at bring she down to my level.  there--don't laugh at I, Jem.'

'why!' he exclaim, impatient of compassion; 'do you think it

'I see your affection give to a most worthy object, and I know what

there could be no objection to the school-room party, and it be no

Isabel be the more provoke with Lord Fitzjocelyn, when, by-and-by, he

CHAPTER XIII.



FROSTY, but KINDLY.



  go not eastward, go not westward,

  for a stranger whom we know not.

  like a fire upon the hearthstone,

  be a neighbour's homely daughter;

  like the moonlight or the starlight,

  be the handsome of stranger.

                      Legend of Hiawatha.





'what a laboured production have the letter be!  how many copy have

'exactly so--good night.'









CHAPTER XIV.



new inhabitant.



  sometimes a troop of damsel glad--

  sometimes a curly shepherd lad,

  or long-haired page in crimson clad,

      go by to tower Camelot;

  and sometimes, through the mirror blue,

  the knight come ride two and two.

  she hath no loyal knight and true--

                          TENNYSON.





'oakstead, Oct. 14th, 1847.



'my dear Aunt,--I find that Fitzjocelyn be write to you, but I think

you will wish for a full account of he than can be obtain from his

own letter.  indeed, I should be much oblige if you would kindly

exercise your influence to persuade he that he be not in a condition

to be imprudent with impunity.  Sir Miles Oakstead be absolutely

shocked to see the alteration in his appearance, as well as in his

spirit; and although both our kind host and hostess be most

solicitous on his account, it happen unfortunately that they be at

this juncture quite alone, so that he be without companion of his own

age.  I must not, however, alarm you.  the fact be, that circumstance

have occur which, though he have act in the most exemplary manner,

have harass and distress he a good deal, and his health suffer

from the difficulty of take sufficient exercise. James will triumph

when he hear that I regret having shorten his stay by the sea-side;

for neither the place nor the weather seem to agree with he: he have

have a recurrence of wakeful night, and be very languid.  poor boy!

yesterday he wander out alone in the rain, lose his way, and come

home so fatigued that he sleep for three hour on the sofa, but to-day

he seem well--have more colour, and have be less silent.  we go to

Leffingham Castle from Monday till Thursday, when I shall take he to

London for Hastings to decide whether it be fit for he to return to

Christchurch after the vacation, accord to his own most anxious

wish.  with my love to Mary Ponsonby and her daughter, and good

remembrance to James,



                                     'your affectionate nephew,

                                               'ORMERSFIELD.'





the same envelope contain another letter of many sheet, begin in

'scene--rose-colour Pastor's Nest.  table, chair, book, paper,

'well, dear Aunt Kitty, to make a transition from the third to the

'October 14th.--Is it in the book of fate that I should always treat

this rose-colour pastor like a carrion crow?  I have do it again!

and it have but bring out more of my father's marvellous kindness and

patience.



'I plunge into the Scotch mist unsuspected and unpursue.  the visible

ebullition of discontent have so much disgusted I that I must need see

whether anything could be do with it, and fairly face the matter, as

I can only do in a walk.  Pillow counsel be feverish and tumultuous;

one be hardly master of oneself.  the soft, cool, mist-laden air, heavy

but incense-breathing, be a far more friendly adjunct in the quiet

not spoil it.  it suit I well than a sunny, glare day, such as

two thing, please my father and myself.  I wish the determination

may last; but mine have never be good for much, and you must help I.



'neither thinking nor fog conduce to see where I be go; and

when my ankle begin to give out, and I be go to turn, I run into a

hedge, which, loom through the mist, I have be take for a fine

range of distant mountain--rather my way of deal with other

see a camera clear itself--blue sky, purple hill, russet and orange

a scarlet maple, a beech grove, skirt a brilliant water meadow, with

a most reflective stream run through it, and give occasion for a

single arched bridge, and a water mill, with a wheel draperie with

white foam; two swan disport on the water (I would not declare they

be not geese), a few cottony flake of mist hang over damp

corner, the hill rise green, with the bright whitewash cottage of

this district, on the side a rich, red, sandstone-colour church, late

architecture, tower rather mouldering--all the more picturesque;

churchyard, all white headstone and ochreous sheep, surmount by a

the whole glowing in the parting coup-de-soleil of a wet day, every

it be a picture that one's mental photograph ought to stamp for life,

and the cheering and interest it give, no one but you can understand. I

wish for you, I know.  it look so poor in word.



'after the service, I lay hold of the urchin whose hearty stare have

believe a word that he say, for I be much well to-day, body and mind.





     Goosey, goosey gander,

     where shall we wander,

'yes--nothing would make Jem so civil to he as his come float in

'well, I most go to my aunt.'

'I be glad you do not come before,' say Mary, heartily; 'I have just

'we like it very much.'

'copying be not compose,' say Mary.



'but you have mend it, make it consecutive!  if I have guess that my

'if you take pain with it, it may be very valuable,' say Mrs.

'go to Oakstead!' say Louis, faintly.

'of course not.  I never stick to my dictionary.'



a sound dose of criticism ensue, temper by repetition of his

supposition.









CHAPTER XVI.



the fruit of the CHRISTMAS-tree



  Age, twine thy brow with fresh spring flower,

  and call a train of laugh hour;

  and bid they dance, and bid they sing:

  and thou, too, mingle in the ring.

                             WORDSWORTH





the 12th of January be the last day before James and Louis mean to

return to Oxford, Jem take Clara on from thence to school.  it be to

be the farewell to Christmas--one much enjoy in Dynevor Terrace.

Fitzjocelyn's absence be almost a relief to Clara; she could not make

up her mind to see he till she could hope their last encounter have

be forget; and in the mean time, her anticipation be fix on

the great 12th.  she be aware of what the entertainment would consist,

but be in honour bind to conceal her knowledge from Virginia and

Louisa, who on their side affect great excitement and curiosity, and

make every ostentation of guess and peep.  gift be smuggle

into the house from every quarter--some to take their chance, some

direct with motto droll or affectionate.  Clara prepare a few

trifle, in which she show that school have do something for her

finger, and commit her little parcel to her brother's care; and

Miss Mercy be the happy of all, continually knock at the locked

door of the back drawing-room with gild fir cone, paint banner,

or moss birds'-nests, from Miss Salome.



Miss King and Isabel have undertake the main business.  when rouse

from her pensive stillness, Isabel could be very eager, active, and

animate; and she work with the exhilaration that she could freely

enjoy when unrestrained by perceive that she be want to produce an

effect.  what woman's height and hand could not perform fall to the

invaluable.  merrily, merrily do the three work, laugh over their

suspend bonbon, their droll contrivance, or predict the

adaptation of their gift; and more and more gay be the laugh, the

tutor more piquant, the governess more keen and clever, the young lady

more vivacious, as the twilight darken, and the tree become more

laden, and the streamer and glass ball produce a more brilliant

effect.



proudly, when the task be accomplish, do they contemplate their

work, and predict the aspect of their tinsel and frippery when duly

light up.  then, as they disperse to dress, James run home, and

hastily tap at his sister's door.



'what be the matter?' she cry.  'have the tassel come off my purse?'



'nothing of the kind, but--' he come quite in, and look round

restlessly, then hastily say, 'you give I nothing for Miss Conway.'



'I wish it very much,' say Clara, 'but I could not bear to do

anything trumpery for she.  oh, if one could give she anything worth

have!'



'Clara, I have think--but I do not know if you would like to part

with it--'



'I have think of it too,' say Clara; 'but I think you would not

like it to be give away.'



pull out a drawer, she open an odd little box of queer

curiosity, whence she take a case contain an exquisite ivory

carving, a copy of the 'Madonna della Sedia,' so fine that a magnifier

alone could fully reveal the delicacy and accuracy of the feature and

expression.  it be mount as a bracelet clasp, and be a remnant of

poor Mr. Dynevor's treasure.  it have be give to Mrs. Henry Frost,

and have descend to her daughter.



'should you be willing?' wistfully ask James.



'that I should!  I have long to give she what she would really care

for.  she have be so very kind--and her kindness be so very sweet in

its graciousness!  I shall always be the happy for the very thinking

of it.'



'I be glad--' begin Jem, warmly; but, break off, he added--'This

would make we all more comfortable.  it would lessen the weight of

obligation, and that would be satisfactory to you.'



'I don't know.  I like people to be so kind, that I cann't feel as if I

would pay they off, but as if I could do nothing but love they.'



'you do not imagine that I rate this as repayment!'



'oh! no, no!'



'no! it be rather that nothing can be too precious--' then

pausing--'You be sure you be willing, Clary?'



'only too glad.  I like it to be something valuable to we as well as in

itself.  if I only have a bit of black velvet, I could set it up.'



in ten minute, Jem have speed to a shop and back again, and stand by

as Clara stitch the clasp to the ribbon velvet; while there be an

amicable dispute, he insist that the envelope should bear only the

initial of the true donor, and she maintain that 'he give the black

velvet.'  she have her way, and write, 'from her grateful C. F. D. and

J. R. F. D.;' and as James take the little packet, he thank she with

an affectionate kiss--a thing so unprecedented at an irregular hour,

that Clara's heart leapt up, and she felt reward for any semblance of

sacrifice.



he tell his grandmother that he have agree with his sister that they

could do no otherwise than present the ivory clasp; and Mrs. Frost, who

have no specially tender association with it, be satisfied to find

that they have anything worth offer on equal term.



she be to be of the party, and set forth, they, find the House

beautiful upside down--even the Faithfull parlour devote to shawl and

bonnet, and the two good old sister in the drawing-room; Miss Salome,

under the protection of little Louisa, in an easy chair, opposite the

respective keeper.  Lady Conway be receive her guest with the

man, Sydney Calcott foremost, former pupil enchant to see she, and

dangerous person to invite, for the Northwold youth have no eye but for

she.



the child be presently take down to tea in the dining-room by

while coffee go round in the drawing-room, Isabel glide out to

perform the lighting process.



'oh, Mr. Dynevor!' she exclaim, find he at her side, 'I do not

mean to call you away.'



'mere unreason to think of the performance alone,' say James, set

'thank you, it may be safe and quick.'



'so far the evening be most successful,' say Jem, light above as

she light below.



'that it be!  I like Northwold well than any place I have be in

friendliness here than in ordinary society.



'I think Fitzjocelyn's open sympathy have conduced--'



Isabel laugh, and he check himself, disconcert.



'I beg your pardon,' she say; 'I be amuse at the force of habit. if

Lord Fitzjocelyn's do.'



'do not bid I do otherwise than keep he in mind.'



down fall the high candle: the hot wax drop on Isabel's arm

hurt you! you be burn!'



'oh no, only startle.  there be no harm do, you see,' as she crack

away the cool wax--'not even a mark to remind I of this happy

Christmas.'



'and it have be a happy Christmas to you,' he say, remounting.



'most happy.  nothing have be so peaceful or satisfactory in my

wandering life.'



'shall I find you here at Easter?'



'I fear not.  Mamma like to be in London early; but perhaps she may

leave the school-room party here, as Louisa be gain so much ground,

and that would be a pledge of our return.'



'too much joy,' say James, almost inaudibly.



'I hope Walter may spend his holiday here,' she pursue.  'it be a

great thing for he to be with any one who can put a few right notion

into his head.'



Jem abstain from, as usual, propose Fitzjocelyn for his example,

but only say that Walter be very susceptible of good impression.



'and most heartily we thank you for all you have do for he,' say

Isabel, doubt whether Walter's mother appreciate the full extent of

it; 'indeed, we have all a great deal to thank you for.  I hope my

sister and I may be the well all our life for the help and

explanation you have give to we.  be that the last candle?  how

beautiful!  we must open.'



'Miss Conway--'



'Yes'--she pause with her hand on the key.



'no, no--do not wait,' take the key himself.  'yet--yes, I must--I

must thank you for such words--'



'my word?' say Isabel, smile.  'for thank you, or be happy

here?'



'Both! both!  those word will be my never-fail charm.  you little

guess how I shall live on the remembrance.  oh, if I could only convey

to you what feeling you have excited--'



the word break from he as if beyond his control, and under the

press need of not waste the taper, he instinctively unlock the

without know what he be about.



instantly Lady Conway and Miss King each push a fold leaf, Isabel

light, glance out among the dark leave, and reflect from the gilt

fir-cone, glass ball, and brilliant toy.



'Sister! sister!' cry Miss Mercy, stand by Miss Faithfull's chair,

in the rear of the throng, and seize her hand in ecstasy; 'it be like

a dream! like what we have read of!  oh, the dear little child!  so

very kind of Lady Conway!  could you have imagined--?' she quite gasp.



'it be very pretty, but it be a nicer Christmas-tree last year at Lady

colour lamp.'



'little fastidious puss!' say Louis, 'I think you keep in the

background out of politeness; but I see you be only blasee with

moment than I could analyse the jewel in Aladdin's cave.'



'oh, if you and Miss Faithfull talk, Cousin Fitzjocelyn, you will make

it seem quite new.'



'you will deride the freshness of our simplicity,' say Louis, but

shine by fairy spell.'



'now, Cousin Fitzjocelyn, do you think I suppose you so silly--'



'Soft!  the Dryad of the Enchanted Bower advance.  her float robe,

her holly crown, beseem her queenly charm.'



'as if you do not know that it be only Isabel!'



'only!  may the word be forgive to a sister!  Isabel!  the name be

all-expressive.'



'she be look even more lovely than usual,' say Miss Faithfull. 'I

never see such a countenance.'



'she have a colour to-night,' add Miss Mercy, 'which do, as you say,

be so sorry I do not help she to light the tree!'



'I do not think it be fatigue,' say her sister.  'I hope it be

'you be as bad as my prosaic cousin,' say Louis, 'disenchant the

'there, cousin, it be you who have call she a modern young lady.'



before Louisa have express her indignation, there be a call for she.

know how to deserve her smile.  Fairy gift remain not with the

as he put she forward, some one make way for she.  it be Mary, and he

'much more comfortable to-night, thank you;' and the pleasant, honest

'I wish she be here.  it be a pretty, more visionary sight than I

'I wish she could see it; but she fear the crowd.  many people in a

'no, it would not be his element.  but imagine his having take to

'really?' say Mary, amuse.

'Mr. Holdsworth tell we how pleased they be.  and the Norrises?'

'oh, that's right.'



'I've be at the plan all the afternoon.  I see how to contrive the

in spite of her willingness to hear the idea, Mary could not help

Mary be answer, when Walter came burst through the crowd. 'where

be he?  Fitzjocelyn, it be your turn.'



'here be a curious speciman for our great naturalist,' say Mrs. Frost,

a glow in her cheek, and her voice all stifle mirth and mischief.



it be a large nest of moss and horsehair, partly conceal under the

low branch, and contain two huge egg streak and spot with

'drop by the parent animal in her flight, on the discovery of the

the Right Honourable Viscount Fitzjocelyn.'



'a fine speciman,' say the Viscount at once, with the air of a

'oh, my dear fellow,' exclaim Sydney, instead of his father; 'you

think it be from a Frosty country.  ha, Clara?'



'not I; I know nothing of it,' cry Clara, in hurry and confusion, not

yet able to be suspect of take liberty with he.



'no?' say Louis, turn about his acquisition; 'I think I know the

Walter and Louisa have bring their mother to see the nest, the point

'I have not courage for it before he,' she laugh.  'as for this

'as a tribute to his imagination?' say Isabel, who, in her mood of

'it would be a curious research,' say Louis, 'whether more of these

'often from want of imagination, and no want of cowardice,' say Isabel.



'that sort of nest have not illuminate egg like these,' say Louis.

he exhibit his mare's nest with his own gay bonhommie to all who be

'what?' ask Louia.



'my ivory clasp with the Madonna,' say Clara.  'Jem and I think it

the only thing worthy of Miss Conway.'



'Hem!' say Louis; 'it be not your fault, Clara; but it would be

graceful to learn to receive a favour.'



'a favour, but not a grand thing like this,' say Clara, show a

beautiful little case of work implement.



'hardly worth, even intrinsically, your mother's bracelet,' say Louis.

'but I be not go to talk treason to the family doctrine, though it

be very inconvenient to your friend.'



'then you think we ought not to have do it?'



'that depend on what I cann't decide.'



'what's that?'



'whether you give it out of love or out of pride.'



'I think we give it out of one, and excuse it by the other.'



'very satisfactory.  to reward you, here be something for you to do. I

shall never get at Aunt Kitty to-night.  I see the midshipman, young

Brewster, will not relinquish she; so will you or will she administer

this letter to the Lady of Eachalott?'



'you don't mean that be Tom Madison!' exclaim Clara.  'why, it be

like copper-plate.  no more fitsgosling!'



'no, indeed!  be he not a clever fellow?  he have just reach the stage

as he turn away in search of Mary, Clara feel a soft hand on her

'I may thank _you_,' say Isabel, in a low, sweet voice, press she

'and Jem,' say Clara; 'he think of it first.'



'it be the most beautiful Christmas gift; but I do not like for you to

'we both wish it, and grandmamma gave leave.  we long for you to

Jem's present too, for he go out and buy the black velvet.'



'clasp it on for I, dear Clara.  there!' and Isabel kiss the finger

Clara's face burn with surprise and pleasure amounting to

Lady Conway be warm in express her hope that Walter would enjoy

Clara's heart be all the light because she be sure that Fitzjocelyn

miss all confidential discussion upon school.



she give Charlotte Tom Madison's letter.  the little maiden take it,

and twirl it about rather superciliously.  'what business have my

very likely he be improve, and she be glad of it, but she know what

be genteel now.  yes, she would read it at once; there be no fear

that it would make her soft and foolish--she have get above that!'









CHAPTER XVII.



the rival.



  'which king, Bezonian?'--Henry IV.





Sir Roland of Provence remain in suspense whether to be a novice or

an irrevocably pledge Hospitalier.  the latter be most probable; and

when Adeline's feeling have be minutely analyse, Miss Conway

discover that she have well not show her morning's work to her

sister.



Clara and Louis pronounce Jem to be as savage as a bear all through

the journey.  Clara declare it be revenge for having be civil and

amiable all through the vacation; and Louis utter a theatrical aside,

that even _that_ could not have be maintain if he have not

occasionally come to Ormersfield to relieve himself a little upon their

two lordship.



Laugh as he might, Fitzjocelyn be much concerned and perplex by his

cousin's ill-humour, when it appear more permanent than could be

puff off in a few ebullition.  attempt to penetrate the gloom make

it heavy, and Louis resolve to give it time to subside.  he wait

have miss his cousin several time before he at length find he alone.



'why, Jem, old fellow, what be you about?  you've not be near my

room this term.  be you renounce I in anticipation of my plucking?'



'you willn't be pluck unless you go out of your sense for the

occasion.'



'no thank to your advice and assistance if I be not.  but it would

conduce to my equanimity, Jem, to know whether we be quarrel, as

in that case I should know how to demean myself.'



'I've no quarrel with you.  you have far more reason--but,' add Jem,

catch himself up, 'don't you know I have no leisure for trifling?

the Ordination be the second week in March.'



'the ordination!'



'ay--you know it!  my fellowship depend on it.'



'I never like to contemplate it.'  he sit down and muse, while James

continue his occupation.  presently he say, 'look here.  Sir Miles

Oakstead ask I if I have any clever Oxford friend to recommend.  if

he come into office, he--'



'I'll be no great man's hanger-on.'



'this matter be not imminent.  you be barely four-and-twenty.  wait a

year or two; even a few month would--'



'you have try my forbearance often enough,' break in James; 'my

object be--as you very well know--to maintain myself and mine without

be liable to obnoxious patronage.  if you think I should disgrace

the office, speak out!'



Louis, without raise his eye, only answer with a smile.



'then, what do you mean?  as to your notion of a vocation, ninety-nine

out of a hundred be in my case.  I have be breed up to this--nothing

else be open--I mean to do my duty; and surely that be vocation--no one

have a right to object--'



'no one; I beg your pardon,' meekly say Louis, take up his stick to

'I have just inclination enough to be able to perceive that you have

'and be every one to follow his bent?'



'this be not a step to be take against the grain, even for the good

would not ask another man live.'



'nor I, if I do not thrust it on you.'



'now for it!  let we hear your objection.'



'simply this.  I do not see that anything impel you to take Holy

if you would but wait a year!'

who be devoid of it: there lie my mean of educate my sister,

provide for my grandmother.  I can see no scruple that should deter
